	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">HBO</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>

		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">American pay television network</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the U.S. pay television channel. For HBO in other countries, see <a href="/wiki/HBO_(international)" title="HBO (international)">HBO (international)</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/HBO_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="HBO (disambiguation)">HBO (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<p><b>HBO</b> (<b>Home Box Office</b>) is an American <a href="/wiki/Pay_television" title="Pay television">premium</a> <a href="/wiki/Television_network" title="Television network">television network</a> owned by Home Box Office, Inc., a subsidiary of <a href="/wiki/WarnerMedia_Entertainment" class="mw-redirect" title="WarnerMedia Entertainment">WarnerMedia Entertainment</a>. The programming featured on the network consists primarily of theatrically released <a href="/wiki/Feature_film" title="Feature film">motion pictures</a> and original television shows, along with made-for-cable movies, documentaries and occasional comedy and concert <a href="/wiki/Television_special" title="Television special">specials</a>.
</p><p>HBO is the oldest and longest continuously operating pay television service (basic or premium) in the United States, having been in operation since November 8, 1972. In 2016, HBO had an adjusted operating income of US$1.93 billion, compared to the US$1.88 billion it accrued in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-BusinessWire2017_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BusinessWire2017-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> HBO has 140 million subscribers worldwide as of 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The network provides seven 24-hour <a href="/wiki/Multiplex_(television)" title="Multiplex (television)">multiplex channels</a>, including HBO Comedy, HBO Latino, HBO Signature, and HBO Family. It launched the streaming service <a href="/wiki/HBO_Now" title="HBO Now">HBO Now</a> in April 2015 and has over 5 million subscribers in the United States as of February&#160;2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-bloombergfeb2018_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloombergfeb2018-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As of July&#160;2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, HBO's programming is available to approximately 36,493,000 households with at least one television set (31.3% of all cable, satellite and telco customers) in the United States (36,013,000 subscribers or 30.9% of all households with pay television service receive at least HBO's primary channel),<sup id="cite_ref-Cable_Subs_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cable_Subs-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> making it the second largest premium channel in the United States (<a href="/wiki/Encore_(TV_network)" class="mw-redirect" title="Encore (TV network)">Encore</a>, owned by <a href="/wiki/Starz_Inc." title="Starz Inc.">Starz Inc.</a>, reaches 40.54 million pay television households as of July&#160;2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>).<sup id="cite_ref-Cable_Subs_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cable_Subs-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> In addition to its U.S. subscriber base, HBO distributes content in at least 151 countries,<sup id="cite_ref-Time_Warner_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Time_Warner-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> with approximately 140 million subscribers worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>HBO subscribers generally pay for an extra tier of service that includes other cable- and satellite-exclusive channels even before paying for the channel itself (though HBO often prices all of its channels together in a single package). However, a regulation imposed by the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Communications_Commission" title="Federal Communications Commission">Federal Communications Commission</a> (FCC) requires that cable providers allow subscribers to get just "limited" basic cable (a base programming tier that includes local, and in some areas, out-of-market <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_television" title="Terrestrial television">broadcast stations</a> and <a href="/wiki/Public,_educational,_and_government_access" title="Public, educational, and government access">public, educational, and government access</a> channels) and premium services such as HBO, without subscribing to expanded service (<a href="/wiki/Comcast" title="Comcast">Comcast</a> is the only major provider to have purposefully offered the network in such a manner utilizing this law, as it offered a <a href="/wiki/Product_bundling" title="Product bundling">bundled</a> cable/Internet package that included limited basic service and HBO from October 2013 to July 2014, or January of the latter year in some markets).<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Cable providers can require the use of a converter box—usually digital—in order to receive HBO.
</p><p>HBO also provides its content through digital media; the channel maintains <a href="/wiki/HBO_Go" title="HBO Go">HBO Go</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Video_on_demand" title="Video on demand">video on demand</a> streaming service available as a website and slate of <a href="/wiki/Mobile_app" title="Mobile app">mobile apps</a> exclusively to existing subscribers of the linear channel suite and a separate, but similar standalone service, <a href="/wiki/HBO_Now" title="HBO Now">HBO Now</a>, which launched in April 2015 as a subscription streaming platform that does not require a subscription to the HBO television service.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gigaom-hbonow_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gigaom-hbonow-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>HBO also maintains near-ubiquitous distribution in hotels across the United States through agreements with <a href="/wiki/DirecTV" title="DirecTV">DirecTV</a>, <a href="/wiki/Echostar" class="mw-redirect" title="Echostar">Echostar</a>, <a href="/wiki/SONIFI_Solutions" class="mw-redirect" title="SONIFI Solutions">SONIFI Solutions</a>, Satellite Management Services, Inc., <a href="/wiki/Itochu" title="Itochu">Telerent Leasing Corporation</a>, Total Media Concepts and World Cinema as well as cable providers that maintain hospitality service arrangements with individual hotels and local franchises of national hotel/motel chains; although Home Box Office Inc. does not keep tallies of national hotel distribution numbers, LodgeNet (now <a href="/wiki/SONIFI_Solutions" class="mw-redirect" title="SONIFI Solutions">SONIFI Solutions</a>) estimated in 2008 that HBO was available to 98% of all hotels that at least receive cable or satellite service via the content and connectivity solutions company. Since June 2018, through a content partnership with Enseo, HBO Go is also distributed to some <a href="/wiki/Marriott_International" title="Marriott International">Marriott International</a> hotels around the U.S.; guests staying in Marriott hotels that have access to HBO Go on connected in-room TV sets are not required to sign into the system in order to access content.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many HBO programs have been <a href="/wiki/Broadcast_syndication" title="Broadcast syndication">syndicated</a> to other networks and broadcast television stations (usually after some editing for running time and/or content that indecency regulations enforced by jurisdictional telecommunications agencies or self-imposed by network <a href="/wiki/Standards_and_Practices" class="mw-redirect" title="Standards and Practices">Standards and Practices</a> departments may prohibit from airing on broadcast and cable networks), and a number of HBO-produced series and films have been released on DVD. Since HBO's more successful series (most notably shows such as <i><a href="/wiki/Sex_and_the_City" title="Sex and the City">Sex and the City</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sopranos" title="The Sopranos">The Sopranos</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wire" title="The Wire">The Wire</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Entourage_(U.S._TV_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="Entourage (U.S. TV series)">Entourage</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Six_Feet_Under_(TV_series)" title="Six Feet Under (TV series)">Six Feet Under</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Boardwalk_Empire" title="Boardwalk Empire">Boardwalk Empire</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Game_of_Thrones" title="Game of Thrones">Game of Thrones</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/True_Blood" title="True Blood">True Blood</a></i>) air on over-the-air broadcasters in other countries (such as in Canada, Australia and much of Europe—including the United Kingdom), HBO's programming has the potential of being exposed to a higher percentage of the population of those countries compared to the United States.
</p><p>Because of the cost of HBO (which is the most expensive of the U.S. premium services, costing a monthly fee as of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> between $15 and $20 depending on the provider), many Americans only view HBO programs through DVDs or in basic cable or broadcast syndication—months or even years after these programs have first aired on the network—and with editing for both content and to allow advertising, although several series have filmed alternate "clean" scenes intended for syndication runs.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_and_launch">Development and launch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Development and launch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1965, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dolan" title="Charles Dolan">Charles Dolan</a>—who had already done pioneering work in the commercial use of <a href="/wiki/Electrical_cable" title="Electrical cable">cables</a> and had developed Teleguide, a <a href="/wiki/Closed-circuit_television" title="Closed-circuit television">closed-circuit</a> <a href="/wiki/Tourist_information" class="mw-redirect" title="Tourist information">tourist information</a> television system distributed to hotels in the <a href="/wiki/New_York_metropolitan_area" title="New York metropolitan area">New York metropolitan area</a>—won a franchise to build a cable television system in the <a href="/wiki/Lower_Manhattan" title="Lower Manhattan">Lower Manhattan</a> section of New York City.<sup id="cite_ref-Sterling_Cable_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sterling_Cable-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The new system, which Dolan named "Sterling Information Services" (later to be known as Sterling Manhattan Cable, and eventually becoming the then <a href="/wiki/Time_Warner_Cable" title="Time Warner Cable">Time Warner Cable</a> which merged into <a href="/wiki/Charter_Communications" title="Charter Communications">Charter Communications</a> in 2016),<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> became the first urban underground cable television system in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Rather than stringing cable on telephone poles or using <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">microwave</a> antennas to receive the signals, Sterling laid cable beneath the streets. This was partly because the tall buildings in the city blocked television signals, and partly because the New York City Council had required that all electrical and telecommunication wiring be laid underground to limit service disruptions during bad weather—an ordinance that was passed after a <a href="/wiki/Great_Blizzard_of_1888" title="Great Blizzard of 1888">blizzard in 1888</a> damaged telephone and <a href="/wiki/Telegraph" class="mw-redirect" title="Telegraph">telegraph</a> lines in the area.<sup id="cite_ref-Green_Channel_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Green_Channel-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> In 1973, <a href="/wiki/Time_Life" title="Time Life">Time-Life, Inc.</a> purchased a 20% stake in Dolan's company.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sterling Manhattan consistently lost money during its first six years of operation, because of both the expense of running cable underground and into buildings throughout Manhattan (as much as $300,000 per mile), and a limited subscriber base—by 1971, subscribers numbered 400. In the summer of that year, while on a family vacation in France, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dolan" title="Charles Dolan">Charles Dolan</a> began to think of ideas to make Sterling Manhattan profitable. He came up with the concept for a cable-originated television service, called "<b>The Green Channel</b>". Dolan later presented his idea to Time-Life management; though <a href="/wiki/Communications_satellite" title="Communications satellite">satellite</a> distribution seemed only a distant possibility at the time, he persuaded Time-Life to back him on the project.<sup id="cite_ref-Green_Channel_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Green_Channel-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>To gauge whether consumers would be interested in subscribing to a pay television service, Time-Life sent out a <a href="/wiki/Direct_mail" class="mw-redirect" title="Direct mail">direct-mail</a> research brochure to residents in six U.S. cities. An overwhelming majority of those surveyed (approximately 99%) opposed the idea; 4% of those polled in a second survey, conducted by an independent consultant, said they were "almost certain" to subscribe to such a service. Time-Life later conducted a test in <a href="/wiki/Allentown,_Pennsylvania" title="Allentown, Pennsylvania">Allentown, Pennsylvania</a>, in which salesmen presented the concept of a pay cable channel to residents by offering free service for the first month and a refundable installation fee; half of residents surveyed in the test expressed interest in purchasing the conceptual service.<sup id="cite_ref-Green_Channel_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Green_Channel-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> In a meeting of Dolan and some Time-Life executives who were working on the project, various other names were discussed for the new service. They ultimately settled on calling it "<b><u>H</u>ome <u>B</u>ox <u>O</u>ffice</b>", although the name was originally intended as a <a href="/wiki/Working_title" title="Working title">working title</a> in order to meet deadlines to publish research brochures for the new service, with the belief that management would come up with a different name later.<sup id="cite_ref-Green_Channel_21-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Green_Channel-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HBO_1972.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/HBO_1972.jpg/170px-HBO_1972.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/HBO_1972.jpg/255px-HBO_1972.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/HBO_1972.jpg/340px-HBO_1972.jpg 2x" data-file-width="460" data-file-height="388" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HBO_1972.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Original HBO logo, used from 1972 to 1975.</div></div></div>
<p>Originally, Home Box Office was to debut on a Teleservice (now <a href="/wiki/Service_Electric" title="Service Electric">Service Electric</a>) cable television system in Allentown; in order to avoid <a href="/wiki/Blackout_(broadcasting)" title="Blackout (broadcasting)">blackouts</a> for <a href="/wiki/National_Basketball_Association" title="National Basketball Association">NBA</a> games that it was set to televise (Allentown was within the NBA's designated blackout radius for the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_76ers" title="Philadelphia 76ers">Philadelphia 76ers</a>' market area, under rules that the league had in effect at the time to protect ticket sales), Time-Life agreed to an offer by Service Electric president <a href="/wiki/John_Walson" title="John Walson">John Walson</a> to launch the channel on its system in <a href="/wiki/Wilkes-Barre,_Pennsylvania" title="Wilkes-Barre, Pennsylvania">Wilkes-Barre</a> (outside of the 76ers' DMA, in <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_Pennsylvania" title="Northeastern Pennsylvania">northeastern Pennsylvania</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Green_Channel_21-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Green_Channel-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Home Box Office launched at 7:30 p.m. <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Time_Zone" title="Eastern Time Zone">Eastern Time</a> on November 8, 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-newspapers-hbodebut_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newspapers-hbodebut-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> However, HBO's launch came without fanfare in the press, as it was not covered by any local or national media outlets, other than through advertisements featured in the <i><a href="/wiki/Times_Leader" title="Times Leader">Wilkes-Barre Times Leader</a></i> promoting the launch. In addition, the city manager of Wilkes-Barre declined an offer to attend the launch ceremony, while Time Inc. president and chief executive officer J. Richard Munro was unable to attend as he was stranded in traffic while trying to exit <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a> on the <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_Bridge" title="George Washington Bridge">George Washington Bridge</a> on his way to Wilkes-Barre.<sup id="cite_ref-Satellite_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Satellite-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The first program and event telecast distributed on the channel, an <a href="/wiki/National_Hockey_League" title="National Hockey League">NHL</a> hockey game between the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Rangers" title="New York Rangers">New York Rangers</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Vancouver_Canucks" title="Vancouver Canucks">Vancouver Canucks</a> from <a href="/wiki/Madison_Square_Garden" title="Madison Square Garden">Madison Square Garden</a>, was transmitted over channel 21—its original assigned channel on the Teleservice system—that evening to 325 Teleservice subscribers in Wilkes-Barre (a plaque commemorating this event is located at Public Square in downtown Wilkes-Barre). Home Box Office distributed its first film immediately after the sports event: the 1971 film <i><a href="/wiki/Sometimes_a_Great_Notion_(film)" title="Sometimes a Great Notion (film)">Sometimes a Great Notion</a></i>, starring <a href="/wiki/Paul_Newman" title="Paul Newman">Paul Newman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Fonda" title="Henry Fonda">Henry Fonda</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-newspapers-hbodebut_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newspapers-hbodebut-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Four months after the channel launched, in February 1973, Home Box Office aired its first non-sports entertainment special, the Pennsylvania Polka Festival<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>, a three-hour-long event broadcast from the <a href="/wiki/Allentown_Fairgrounds" title="Allentown Fairgrounds">Allentown Fairgrounds</a> in Allentown, Pennsylvania.<sup id="cite_ref-An_Original_Voice_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-An_Original_Voice-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Home Box Office would use a network of microwave relay towers to distribute its programming to cable systems throughout its service area.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forsyth_2002_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsyth_2002-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sterling Manhattan Cable continued to lose money because the company had only a small subscriber base of 20,000 customers in Manhattan. Dolan's media partner, Time-Life, Inc., gained control of Sterling when it acquired an additional 60% equity interest, increasing its stake in the company to 80%; Time-Life then decided to close the Sterling Manhattan operation. Time-Life dropped the "Sterling" name and the company was renamed "Manhattan Cable Television" under Time-Life's control in March 1973.<sup id="cite_ref-Sterling_Cable_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sterling_Cable-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gerald_M._Levin" title="Gerald M. Levin">Gerald Levin</a>, who had been with Home Box Office since it began operations as its vice president of programming, replaced Dolan as the company's president and chief executive officer.<sup id="cite_ref-HBO_70s_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBO_70s-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 1973, Time-Life, Inc. completed its acquisition of the pay service. At the time, Home Box Office's future looked dim: it only had 8,000 subscribers across 14 cable systems, all of which were located in Pennsylvania,<sup id="cite_ref-Satellite_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Satellite-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> and it was suffering from a significant subscriber <a href="/wiki/Churn_rate" title="Churn rate">churn rate</a>. HBO would eventually increase its fortunes within two years: by April 1975, the service had around 100,000 subscribers in Pennsylvania and <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York state</a>, and had begun to turn a limited profit.<sup id="cite_ref-Satellite_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Satellite-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="National_expansion.2C_innovation_and_rise_to_prominence_.281975.E2.80.931993.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="National_expansion,_innovation_and_rise_to_prominence_(1975–1993)">National expansion, innovation and rise to prominence (1975–1993)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: National expansion, innovation and rise to prominence (1975–1993)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Satcom,_Communication_Satellite_-_GPN-2003-00010.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Satcom%2C_Communication_Satellite_-_GPN-2003-00010.jpg/170px-Satcom%2C_Communication_Satellite_-_GPN-2003-00010.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="214" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Satcom%2C_Communication_Satellite_-_GPN-2003-00010.jpg/255px-Satcom%2C_Communication_Satellite_-_GPN-2003-00010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Satcom%2C_Communication_Satellite_-_GPN-2003-00010.jpg/340px-Satcom%2C_Communication_Satellite_-_GPN-2003-00010.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2260" data-file-height="2845" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Satcom,_Communication_Satellite_-_GPN-2003-00010.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The RCA Satcom domestic communication satellite launched on December 13, 1975, spurred the cable television industry to unprecedented heights – with the assistance of HBO.</div></div></div>
<p>Time-Life executives realized the problems in trying to expand Home Box Office's distribution footprint using microwave towers because of the time and expense that would be incurred in developing such a vast relay infrastructure, and began looking for cost-efficient methods of transmitting the channel nationally. In 1974, they settled on using a geostationary <a href="/wiki/Communications_satellite" title="Communications satellite">communications satellite</a> to transmit HBO to cable providers throughout the United States. Other television broadcasters at the time were hesitant about uplinking their feeds to satellite due to fears that the satellites may inadvertently shut down or jettison out of their orbit, as well as due to the cost of purchasing downlink receiver dishes, which in 1974, were sold for as much as $75,000. Seeing satellite transmission as the only viable option to expand HBO's reach, Gerald Levin allocated $6.5 million to lease <a href="/wiki/Transponder" title="Transponder">transponder</a> space on the <a href="/wiki/Westar_1" title="Westar 1">Westar 1</a> satellite for a five-year term.<sup id="cite_ref-Satellite_26-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Satellite-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The Time-Life board subsequently approved the plan to transmit HBO via satellite.<sup id="cite_ref-HBO_70s_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBO_70s-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At 9:00 p.m. <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Time_Zone" title="Eastern Time Zone">Eastern Time</a> on September 30, 1975, HBO became the first television network to continuously deliver its signal via satellite (as opposed to microwave relay, the industry norm at the time) when it distributed the "<a href="/wiki/Thrilla_in_Manila" title="Thrilla in Manila">Thrilla in Manila</a>" <a href="/wiki/Boxing" title="Boxing">boxing</a> match between <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali" title="Muhammad Ali">Muhammad Ali</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joe_Frazier" title="Joe Frazier">Joe Frazier</a>; it was beamed to UA Columbia Cablevision's systems in <a href="/wiki/Fort_Pierce,_Florida" title="Fort Pierce, Florida">Fort Pierce</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vero_Beach,_Florida" title="Vero Beach, Florida">Vero Beach, Florida</a>, and American Television and Communications Corporation's <a href="/wiki/Jackson,_Mississippi" title="Jackson, Mississippi">Jackson, Mississippi</a> system,<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> as well as those already carrying HBO in the northeastern United States.<sup id="cite_ref-Satellite_26-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Satellite-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forsyth_2002_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsyth_2002-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Through the use of satellite, the channel began transmitting separate programming feeds for the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Time_Zone" title="Eastern Time Zone">Eastern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Time_Zone" title="Pacific Time Zone">Pacific Time Zones</a>, allowing the same programs that air first in the eastern half of the United States to air at accordant times in the western part of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-ExpandingtheBrand2_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ExpandingtheBrand2-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> HBO switched its domestic satellite transmissions from Westar 1 to <a href="/wiki/Satcom_(satellite)" title="Satcom (satellite)">Satcom 1</a> in February 1976. By 1977, <a href="/wiki/Ted_Turner" title="Ted Turner">Ted Turner</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Atlanta" title="Atlanta">Atlanta</a> <a href="/wiki/Superstation" title="Superstation">superstation</a> <a href="/wiki/WPCH-TV" title="WPCH-TV">WTCG-TV</a> (soon to become <a href="/wiki/TBS_(U.S._TV_channel)" class="mw-redirect" title="TBS (U.S. TV channel)">WTBS</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Pat_Robertson" title="Pat Robertson">Pat Robertson</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Television_networks_preceding_Freeform#CBN_Satellite_Service/Cable_Network" class="mw-redirect" title="Television networks preceding Freeform">CBN Satellite Service</a> (later to become the present-day <a href="/wiki/Freeform_(TV_channel)" title="Freeform (TV channel)">Freeform</a>) had joined it, pioneering satellite delivery for the cable television industry.<sup id="cite_ref-Forsyth_2002_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsyth_2002-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> By 1980, HBO was carried on cable providers in all 50 U.S. states.<sup id="cite_ref-Satellite_26-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Satellite-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HBO_logo_1975.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/HBO_logo_1975.png/140px-HBO_logo_1975.png" decoding="async" width="140" height="83" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/HBO_logo_1975.png/210px-HBO_logo_1975.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/HBO_logo_1975.png/280px-HBO_logo_1975.png 2x" data-file-width="577" data-file-height="342" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HBO_logo_1975.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>First version of HBO's current<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> logo, used from 1975 to 1981; during 1980, HBO used this logo in tandem with the second incarnation of the logo (seen above, in the Infobox) that is still used to this day<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>.</div></div></div>
<p>HBO distributed its programming for only nine hours each day, from 3:00 p.m. to 12:00 am. Eastern Time, during its first nine years of operation. The network first adopted a 24-hour schedule on weekends on September 4, 1981, running from 3:00 p.m. on Friday afternoons until 12:00 am. Eastern Time on Sunday nights/early Monday mornings; this round-the-clock schedule was expanded to weekdays three months later on December 28, 1981 (however, HBO was not the first pay television network to maintain an uninterrupted programming schedule as <a href="/wiki/Showtime_(TV_network)" title="Showtime (TV network)">Showtime</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Movie_Channel" title="The Movie Channel">The Movie Channel</a> had both switched to 24-hour daily schedules months earlier). By this time, the full "Home Box Office" name was de-emphasized by the network, in favor of branding solely by the "HBO" <a href="/wiki/Initialism" class="mw-redirect" title="Initialism">initialism</a> (although the full name is still used as the legal corporate name of its parent division under <a href="/wiki/WarnerMedia" title="WarnerMedia">WarnerMedia</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1983, HBO premiered its first original movie, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Terry_Fox_Story" title="The Terry Fox Story">The Terry Fox Story</a></i>, a biographical film about the <a href="/wiki/Terry_Fox" title="Terry Fox">Canadian runner</a> who embarked on a cross-country run across Canada to raise money and awareness for cancer research; the film was also the first movie ever produced for pay television. That year also saw the premiere of the first children's program to be distributed on the channel: <i><a href="/wiki/Fraggle_Rock" title="Fraggle Rock">Fraggle Rock</a></i> (that series' creator, <a href="/wiki/Jim_Henson" title="Jim Henson">Jim Henson</a>, had earlier produced the special <i><a href="/wiki/Emmet_Otter%27s_Jug-Band_Christmas_(TV_program)" class="mw-redirect" title="Emmet Otter&#39;s Jug-Band Christmas (TV program)">Emmet Otter's Jug-Band Christmas</a></i>, which won an <a href="/wiki/CableACE_Awards" class="mw-redirect" title="CableACE Awards">ACE Award</a> in 1978<sup id="cite_ref-An_Original_Voice_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-An_Original_Voice-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>). HBO continued to air various original programs aimed at children until 2001, when these programs almost completely moved over to HBO Family (which continued to occasionally distribute its own slate of original children's programming until 2003).<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>HBO became involved in several <a href="/wiki/Lawsuit" title="Lawsuit">lawsuits</a> during the 1980s, involving legal statutes imposed by state and city laws that would have resulted in some programs on HBO and other pay television networks being <a href="/wiki/Censorship" title="Censorship">censored</a> by cable systems, if not forcing the pay services to edit inappropriate content from the programming they aired. In January 1986, HBO became the first satellite-delivered television network to <a href="/wiki/Television_encryption" title="Television encryption">encrypt</a> its signal from unauthorized viewing by way of the <a href="/wiki/Videocipher" title="Videocipher">Videocipher</a> II system; this initially resulted in a mass lodge of complaints from <a href="/wiki/Television_receive-only" title="Television receive-only">television receive-only</a> (TVRO) satellite users that previously received HBO's programming without a subscription.<sup id="cite_ref-ExpandingtheBrand2_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ExpandingtheBrand2-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> The objections by TVRO users over having to now pay for HBO as cable subscribers had long done (requiring dish subscribers to purchase an expensive descrambler to unencrypt the signal) came to a head four months later, as HBO became a victim of <a href="/wiki/Broadcast_signal_intrusion" title="Broadcast signal intrusion">broadcast signal intrusion</a> when satellite television dealer John R. MacDougall, a Florida man calling himself "<a href="/wiki/Captain_Midnight_(HBO)" class="mw-redirect" title="Captain Midnight (HBO)">Captain Midnight</a>", redirected a receiver dish towards the network's transponder on Galaxy 1 and intercepted its signal during a movie presentation of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Falcon_and_the_Snowman" title="The Falcon and the Snowman">The Falcon and the Snowman</a></i>; MacDougall overrode the telecast of the film with a text-based message placed over <a href="/wiki/SMPTE_color_bars" title="SMPTE color bars">SMPTE color bars</a> in protest of the channel's decision to scramble its signal for home satellite subscribers. The Federal Communications Commission subsequently prosecuted MacDougall for committing the intrusion.
</p><p>In 1988, HBO's subscriber base expanded greatly as a result of the <a href="/wiki/Writers_Guild_of_America" title="Writers Guild of America">Writers Guild of America</a> <a href="/wiki/1988_Writers_Guild_of_America_strike" title="1988 Writers Guild of America strike">strike</a> that year, as the channel had new programming in its inventory during a period in which the broadcast networks were only able to air <a href="/wiki/Rerun" title="Rerun">reruns</a> of their shows. In 1989, HBO <a href="/wiki/Comparative_advertising" title="Comparative advertising">compared</a> its programming against rival pay television network Showtime, with the slogan "Simply the Best", using the <a href="/wiki/Tina_Turner" title="Tina Turner">Tina Turner</a> single "<a href="/wiki/The_Best_(song)" title="The Best (song)">The Best</a>" as part of the network's on-air image campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On January 2, 1989, HBO launched Selecciones en Español de HBO y Cinemax ("<i>Spanish Selections from HBO and Cinemax</i>")—an alternate Spanish-language feed of HBO and Cinemax. The service, which initially launched on 20 cable systems in <a href="/wiki/Media_market" title="Media market">markets</a> with significant populations of Spanish speakers,<sup id="cite_ref-HBO_Expanding_the_Brand_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBO_Expanding_the_Brand-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> originally only carried Spanish audio simulcasts of live boxing matches televised by HBO (except for certain events that were already distributed in Spanish on networks such as <a href="/wiki/Galavisi%C3%B3n_(USA)" class="mw-redirect" title="Galavisión (USA)">Galavisión</a>), dubbed versions of recent feature film releases from HBO's movie suppliers and first-run Spanish-language movies (mostly from Mexico, Argentina and Spain), but later added Spanish dubs of films and other programs distributed by HBO. Selecciones—which was offered in tandem with HBO, although it operated as a separate service—utilized the <a href="/wiki/Second_audio_program" title="Second audio program">second audio program</a> auxiliary channel to distribute its Spanish audio feeds. Selecciones en Español de HBO y Cinemax became successful to the point that it added 35 additional cable systems to its list of carriers within a few weeks after its debut.<sup id="cite_ref-HBO_Expanding_the_Brand_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBO_Expanding_the_Brand-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Selecciones en Español became HBO en Español on September 27, 1993.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Taking advantage of HBO's successes, <a href="/wiki/Warner_Communications" class="mw-redirect" title="Warner Communications">Warner Communications</a> (which ironically was part-owner of one of the network's pay-cable competitors, The Movie Channel, from its launch in 1973 until joint venture group <a href="/wiki/Warner-Amex_Satellite_Entertainment" title="Warner-Amex Satellite Entertainment">Warner-Amex Satellite Entertainment</a> sold its stake in the channel to <a href="/wiki/Viacom_(original)" class="mw-redirect" title="Viacom (original)">Viacom</a> in 1986) merged with HBO parent <a href="/wiki/Time_Inc." title="Time Inc.">Time Inc.</a> in 1989 to create Time Warner (now known as <a href="/wiki/WarnerMedia" title="WarnerMedia">WarnerMedia</a>), which as of 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, remains the parent company of the network.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> In 1991, HBO and Cinemax became the first premium services to offer <a href="/wiki/Multiplex_(TV)" class="mw-redirect" title="Multiplex (TV)">multiplexed</a> channels to cable customers with the launches of HBO2, HBO3 and Cinemax 2 on three cable systems in <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kansas" title="Kansas">Kansas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> In 1993, HBO became the world's first digitally transmitted television service.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> The move proved successful, eventually resulting in HBO and Cinemax starting up additional multiplex channels of both services—starting with the December 1996 launch of HBO Family and concluding with the launch of four Cinemax channels in 2001: WMax (now MovieMax), @Max (now Cinemáx), OuterMax and 5StarMax.
</p>
<h3><span id="Rising_prominence_of_original_programming_.281993.E2.80.932018.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Rising_prominence_of_original_programming_(1993–2018)">Rising prominence of original programming (1993–2018)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Rising prominence of original programming (1993–2018)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HBO_Headquarters.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/HBO_Headquarters.jpg/275px-HBO_Headquarters.jpg" decoding="async" width="275" height="367" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/HBO_Headquarters.jpg/413px-HBO_Headquarters.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/HBO_Headquarters.jpg/550px-HBO_Headquarters.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HBO_Headquarters.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>HBO headquarters in New York City, United States, April 2017.</div></div></div>
<p>During the 1990s, HBO began to experience increasing success with its original series such as <i><a href="/wiki/Tales_from_the_Crypt_(TV_series)" title="Tales from the Crypt (TV series)">Tales from the Crypt</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Dream_On_(TV_series)" title="Dream On (TV series)">Dream On</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Tracey_Takes_On..." title="Tracey Takes On...">Tracey Takes On...</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Mr._Show_with_Bob_and_David" title="Mr. Show with Bob and David">Mr. Show with Bob and David</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Arliss" title="Arliss">Arliss</a></i>. One such program, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Larry_Sanders_Show" title="The Larry Sanders Show">The Larry Sanders Show</a></i>, arguably became HBO's flagship series during that decade and although it was not commercially as successful as programs that aired on the <a href="/wiki/Big_Three_television_networks" title="Big Three television networks">Big Three networks</a> (<a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting_Company" title="American Broadcasting Company">ABC</a>, <a href="/wiki/NBC" title="NBC">NBC</a> and <a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">CBS</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Fox_Broadcasting_Company" title="Fox Broadcasting Company">Fox</a>, the show did enjoy a cult status and critical acclaim, and received nominations and wins for many major television awards (including <a href="/wiki/Primetime_Emmy_Award" title="Primetime Emmy Award">Primetime Emmy Awards</a> and <a href="/wiki/Golden_Globe_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="Golden Globe Award">Golden Globe Awards</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-imdbawards_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-imdbawards-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The series ranked No. 38 on <a href="/wiki/TV_Guide%27s_50_Greatest_TV_Shows_of_All_Time" title="TV Guide&#39;s 50 Greatest TV Shows of All Time">TV Guide's 50 Greatest TV Shows of All Time</a> (becoming the only HBO comedy series to make the list)<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> and was also included in <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s list of the "100 Best TV Shows of All Time".<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Larry Sanders Show</i> was also ranked by various critics and fans as one of the best TV comedies of the 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The original programs that HBO has developed since the early 1990s have earned the channel numerous nominations and wins at the Emmy and Golden Globe Awards.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Two reasons for what is perceived as the higher quality of these shows are the quality of the writing on the programs and the fact that as a subscription-only service, HBO does not carry "normal" commercials; instead the network runs <a href="/wiki/Promo_(media)" title="Promo (media)">promotions</a> for upcoming HBO programs and behind-the-scenes featurettes between programs. This relieves HBO from some pressures to tone down controversial aspects of its programs, and allows for more explicit content to be incorporated into its shows that would not be allowed to air on broadcast television or basic cable, such as profanity, strong/graphic violence, nudity and graphic sex scenes.
</p><p>Beginning with the 1997 launch of its first one-hour dramatic narrative series <i><a href="/wiki/Oz_(TV_series)" title="Oz (TV series)">Oz</a></i>, HBO started a trend that became commonplace with premium cable services. While the show was critically acclaimed, it was not until <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sopranos" title="The Sopranos">The Sopranos</a></i> premiered in 1999, that the network achieved widespread critical success for an hour-long series. <i>The Sopranos</i>—centering on mob patriarch <a href="/wiki/Tony_Soprano" title="Tony Soprano">Tony Soprano</a> (<a href="/wiki/James_Gandolfini" title="James Gandolfini">James Gandolfini</a>) and his family—received 111 Emmy nominations during its six-season run, resulting in 21 wins, two of them for <a href="/wiki/Primetime_Emmy_Award_for_Outstanding_Drama_Series" title="Primetime Emmy Award for Outstanding Drama Series">Outstanding Drama Series</a>.
</p><p>1998 saw the debut of <i><a href="/wiki/From_the_Earth_to_the_Moon_(miniseries)" title="From the Earth to the Moon (miniseries)">From the Earth to the Moon</a></i>, a 12-part <a href="/wiki/Miniseries" title="Miniseries">miniseries</a> that was produced by <a href="/wiki/Tom_Hanks" title="Tom Hanks">Tom Hanks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ron_Howard" title="Ron Howard">Ron Howard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brian_Grazer" title="Brian Grazer">Brian Grazer</a> and based on the <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Chaikin" title="Andrew Chaikin">Andrew Chaikin</a> book <i><a href="/wiki/A_Man_on_the_Moon" title="A Man on the Moon">A Man on the Moon</a></i>. Costing $68 million to produce, it traced the U.S. space program from the U.S./U.S.S.R. space race through the final moon landing, <a href="/wiki/Apollo_17" title="Apollo 17">Apollo 17</a>. <i>From the Earth to the Moon</i> won an Emmy Award for <a href="/wiki/Primetime_Emmy_Award_for_Outstanding_Drama_Series" title="Primetime Emmy Award for Outstanding Drama Series">Outstanding Miniseries</a>, and helped spur other HBO miniseries based on historical events such as <i><a href="/wiki/61*" title="61*">61*</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Band_of_Brothers_(miniseries)" title="Band of Brothers (miniseries)">Band of Brothers</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/John_Adams_(miniseries)" title="John Adams (miniseries)">John Adams</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Pacific_(miniseries)" title="The Pacific (miniseries)">The Pacific</a></i>. That year also saw the debut of the comedy series <i><a href="/wiki/Sex_and_the_City" title="Sex and the City">Sex and the City</a></i>, which was based on the book series of the same name by <a href="/wiki/Candace_Bushnell" title="Candace Bushnell">Candace Bushnell</a>; over the course of its six-season run, the show—centering on the friendship and romances of four New York City women<sup id="cite_ref-HBOGoldenAge3_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBOGoldenAge3-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>—received 54 Emmy nominations, winning seven, including one win for <a href="/wiki/Primetime_Emmy_Award_for_Outstanding_Comedy_Series" title="Primetime Emmy Award for Outstanding Comedy Series">Outstanding Comedy Series</a>.
</p><p>In 1999, HBO became the first U.S. cable channel to operate a <a href="/wiki/High-definition_television" title="High-definition television">high-definition</a> simulcast feed.<sup id="cite_ref-HBO_HD_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBO_HD-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> In July 2001, HBO launched HBO on Demand, the first premium <a href="/wiki/Video_on_demand" title="Video on demand">subscription video-on-demand</a> enhancement in the United States, to Time Warner Cable subscribers in <a href="/wiki/Columbia,_South_Carolina" title="Columbia, South Carolina">Columbia, South Carolina</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HBO_On_Demand_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBO_On_Demand-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2001, HBO debuted <i><a href="/wiki/Six_Feet_Under_(TV_series)" title="Six Feet Under (TV series)">Six Feet Under</a></i> and in 2002 <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wire" title="The Wire">The Wire</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> which, although not surpassing <i>The Sopranos</i> in viewership success, matched its critical acclaim and further cemented HBO's reputation as a network that produced quality programming.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> HBO experienced another success among viewers in 2008, with the debut of <i><a href="/wiki/True_Blood" title="True Blood">True Blood</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Vampire" title="Vampire">vampire</a> drama based on a series of gothic novels by <a href="/wiki/Charlaine_Harris" title="Charlaine Harris">Charlaine Harris</a>. The network saw three more hit series in the 2010s with <i><a href="/wiki/Game_of_Thrones" title="Game of Thrones">Game of Thrones</a></i>, based on <a href="/wiki/George_R._R._Martin" title="George R. R. Martin">George R. R. Martin</a>'s fantasy novel series <i><a href="/wiki/A_Song_of_Ice_and_Fire" title="A Song of Ice and Fire">A Song of Ice and Fire</a></i>, which earned both critical and viewer praise; <i><a href="/wiki/Girls_(TV_series)" title="Girls (TV series)">Girls</a></i>, a comedy series created by series star <a href="/wiki/Lena_Dunham" title="Lena Dunham">Lena Dunham</a>; and <i><a href="/wiki/True_Detective_(TV_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="True Detective (TV series)">True Detective</a></i>, an <a href="/wiki/Anthology_series" title="Anthology series">anthology</a>-style series—structured to feature a different cast and setting within each season's storyline—which initially saw established film actors <a href="/wiki/Woody_Harrelson" title="Woody Harrelson">Woody Harrelson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Matthew_McConaughey" title="Matthew McConaughey">Matthew McConaughey</a> in its lead roles.<sup id="cite_ref-Fall_and_Rise_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fall_and_Rise-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On August 13, 2015, HBO announced its re-entry into children's programming, when it reached a five-year programming and development deal with <a href="/wiki/Sesame_Workshop" title="Sesame Workshop">Sesame Workshop</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Through the agreement, HBO obtained first-run television rights to <i><a href="/wiki/Sesame_Street" title="Sesame Street">Sesame Street</a></i>, beginning with the January 2016 debut of its 46th season (with episodes being distributed to the program's longtime broadcaster, <a href="/wiki/PBS" title="PBS">PBS</a>, following a nine-month exclusivity window at no charge to its member stations); Sesame Workshop will also produce original children's programming content for the channel, which will also gain exclusive streaming rights to the company's programming library for HBO Go and HBO Now (assuming those rights from <a href="/wiki/Amazon_Video" class="mw-redirect" title="Amazon Video">Amazon Video</a>, <a href="/wiki/Netflix" title="Netflix">Netflix</a> and Sesame Workshop's in-house subscription streaming service, Sesame Go, the latter of which will cease to operate as a standalone offering). Although struck with the intent to having the show remain on PBS in some fashion, the nonprofit <a href="/wiki/Production_company" title="Production company">production company</a> reached the deal due to cutbacks resulting from declines in public and private donations, distribution fees paid by PBS member stations and licensing for merchandise sales.<sup id="cite_ref-cnnmoney-sesamestreet_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnnmoney-sesamestreet-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In August 2017, HBO and <a href="/wiki/Sky_plc" class="mw-redirect" title="Sky plc">Sky</a> took minority stakes in British <a href="/wiki/Production_company" title="Production company">TV Production Company</a> <i>Bad Wolf</i> (The Night Of), as a part of their co-production deal.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="AT.26T_era_.282018.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="AT&amp;T_era_(2018–present)">AT&amp;T era (2018–present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: AT&amp;T era (2018–present)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On October 22, 2016, <a href="/wiki/AT%26T" title="AT&amp;T">AT&amp;T</a> announced an offer to acquire <a href="/wiki/Time_Warner" class="mw-redirect" title="Time Warner">Time Warner</a> for $108.7 billion, including debt it would assume from the latter.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The purchase was completed in June 2018. The proposed merger was confirmed on June 12, 2018, after AT&amp;T won an <a href="/wiki/Antitrust" class="mw-redirect" title="Antitrust">antitrust</a> lawsuit that the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">U.S. Justice Department</a> filed in 2017 to attempt to block the acquisition. The merger closed two days later, with the company becoming a wholly owned subsidiary of AT&amp;T under the renamed parent company <a href="/wiki/WarnerMedia" title="WarnerMedia">WarnerMedia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In July 2018, <i>The New York Times</i> obtained audio from a corporate <a href="/wiki/Town_hall" title="Town hall">town hall</a> meeting featuring AT&amp;T executive John Stankey – WarnerMedia CEO since the merger's completion – who argued that HBO's current content model was not profitable enough, and that the network had to produce more content (similar to that offered by streaming services such as <a href="/wiki/Netflix" title="Netflix">Netflix</a>) in order to achieve more engagement with subscribers (including short-form content oriented towards mobile devices), and that HBO needed to "move beyond 35 to 40 percent penetration to have this become a much more common product." Stankey's statement contradicted the fact that HBO had been consistently profitable over the last three years, totaling nearly $6 billion, while allocating more than $2 billion per year for programming.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On September 27, 2018, HBO announced that it would discontinue its telecasts of boxing after 45 years, citing diminishing ratings, increased competition, and its shift in programming direction.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On October 10, 2018, WarnerMedia CEO John Stankey stated that the company planned on introducing a <a href="/wiki/HBO_Max" title="HBO Max">new streaming service</a> in the future, that would feature content from HBO, Turner, and Warner Bros.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On November 1, 2018, HBO was permanently pulled from <a href="/wiki/Dish_Network" title="Dish Network">Dish Network</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sling_TV" title="Sling TV">Sling TV</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Carriage_dispute" title="Carriage dispute">carriage dispute</a>, marking the first time the network had ever had to pull itself from a provider in its 46-year history.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On February 28, 2019, Plepler stepped down from his position as CEO. <i>The New York Times</i> reported that Plepler "found he had less autonomy after the merger."<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> On March 4, WarnerMedia announced a reorganization, which dissolved <a href="/wiki/Turner_Broadcasting" class="mw-redirect" title="Turner Broadcasting">Turner Broadcasting</a> and replaced it with WarnerMedia Entertainment, led by former NBC executive <a href="/wiki/Bob_Greenblatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Bob Greenblatt">Bob Greenblatt</a>, which consists of HBO and its basic cable siblings <a href="/wiki/TBS_(U.S._TV_channel)" class="mw-redirect" title="TBS (U.S. TV channel)">TBS</a>, <a href="/wiki/TNT_(U.S._TV_network)" class="mw-redirect" title="TNT (U.S. TV network)">TNT</a>, and <a href="/wiki/TruTV" title="TruTV">TruTV</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Channels">Channels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Channels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1991, HBO and Cinemax became the first premium services to offer multiplexed services to cable customers as companions to the main network, offering additional channels of the two services to three TeleCable-operated systems in <a href="/wiki/Overland_Park,_Kansas" title="Overland Park, Kansas">Overland Park, Kansas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Racine,_Wisconsin" title="Racine, Wisconsin">Racine, Wisconsin</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Dallas" title="Dallas">Dallas</a> suburbs of <a href="/wiki/Richardson,_Texas" title="Richardson, Texas">Richardson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plano,_Texas" title="Plano, Texas">Plano, Texas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> The first two multiplex channels—HBO2 and HBO3—launched as part of this test by the TeleCable systems. The following year, research from <a href="/wiki/ACNielsen" class="mw-redirect" title="ACNielsen">ACNielsen</a> revealed that multiplex delivery of HBO and Cinemax had a positive impact on subscriber usage and attitudes, including aiding in the retention of pay cable subscriptions by its subscribers.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The HBO multiplex would later expand in December 1996, with the launch of HBO Family, focusing on family-oriented feature films and television series aimed at younger children.<sup id="cite_ref-HBO_Family_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBO_Family-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> The HBO multiplex channels became collectively marketed under the brand name "HBO The Works" in April 1998 (the Cinemax channels concurrently began to be marketed as "MultiMax"), coinciding with the name change of HBO2 as HBO Plus (the channel would ultimately restore the HBO2 name in September 2002), and the <a href="/wiki/Rebranding" title="Rebranding">rebranding</a> and format change of HBO3 as HBO Signature (a network aimed at women). In May 1999, two more channels launched: HBO Comedy (featuring comedic films and series, along with comedy specials) and HBO Zone (a network aimed at young adults).<sup id="cite_ref-HBO_digital_networks_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBO_digital_networks-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Just over a year later in October 2000, the Spanish language network HBO Latino debuted, featuring a mix of dubbed simulcasts of the main HBO channel's programming and Spanish programs that are exclusive to the channel.
</p><p>"HBO The Works" continued in use as the brand for the HBO multiplex until 2004. Individually, the HBO multiplex channel suite has no "official" marketed name as of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, although HBO and Cinemax's respective multiplex packages are marketed collectively as the "HBO/MAX Pak".<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> HBO Family and HBO Latino had the distinction of being the only multiplex channels of HBO that have their own websites, as all of the others were integrated within the main HBO site; the separate sites and sections for both channels were eliminated in 2010, around the time the HBO Go service was launched.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_channels">List of channels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: List of channels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Depending on the service provider, HBO provides up to fourteen multiplex channels—seven 24-hour multiplex channels, all of which are simulcast in both <a href="/wiki/Standard-definition_television" title="Standard-definition television">standard definition</a> and <a href="/wiki/High-definition_television" title="High-definition television">high definition</a>—as well as a <a href="/wiki/Video_on_demand" title="Video on demand">subscription video-on-demand</a> service (HBO on Demand). Off-the-air maintenance periods of a half-hour up to two hours occur during overnight periods at scattered times on each channel once each month.
</p><p>HBO broadcasts its primary and multiplex channels on both Eastern and Pacific Time Zone schedules. The respective coastal feeds of each channel are usually packaged together (though most cable providers only offer the east and west coast feeds of the main HBO channel, as well as HBO2 in some cases), resulting in the <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_time_zones_on_North_American_broadcasting" title="Effects of time zones on North American broadcasting">difference in local airtimes</a> for a particular movie or program between two geographic locations being three hours at most. The premium film service Cinemax, which is also owned by WarnerMedia through Home Box Office Inc., operates as a separate service from HBO; although HBO is very frequently sold together in a package with Cinemax, subscribers to one of the services do not necessarily have to subscribe to the other.
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center; background:#fff; width:100%;">

<tbody><tr style="color:white;">
<th style="background:darkRed;">Channel
</th>
<th style="background:darkRed;">Description and programming
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:center; width:130pt;"><a href="/wiki/File:HBO_logo.svg" class="image"><img alt="HBO logo.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/HBO_logo.svg/125px-HBO_logo.svg.png" decoding="async" width="125" height="52" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/HBO_logo.svg/188px-HBO_logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/HBO_logo.svg/250px-HBO_logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="422" data-file-height="174" /></a><br /><b>HBO</b></td>
<td style="text-align:left;">The <a href="/wiki/Flagship_(broadcasting)" title="Flagship (broadcasting)">flagship</a> service; HBO airs popular feature films, first-run films, original series and made-for-cable movies, boxing events, sports, comedy and occasional concert specials, and documentaries. The channel also typically debuts new movies on a weekly basis—with feature films debuting on HBO within a lag of between eight months to one year on average from their initial theatrical release—on Saturday nights (usually around 8:00 pm. Eastern Time; the Pacific Time Zone broadcast of the premiered film airs later in the evening when a live special—most commonly, a telecast of <i><a href="/wiki/HBO_World_Championship_Boxing" title="HBO World Championship Boxing">HBO World Championship Boxing</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/HBO_Boxing_After_Dark" class="mw-redirect" title="HBO Boxing After Dark">HBO Boxing After Dark</a></i>—is scheduled to air that particular Saturday, with the special being shown after the movie on the Eastern Time Zone feed). The main HBO channel mainly airs R-rated films only after 8:00 pm. Eastern and Pacific, but does air certain TV-MA rated programs during the daytime hours.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:center; width:130pt;"><a href="/wiki/File:HBO_2_Logo.png" class="image"><img alt="HBO 2 Logo.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/HBO_2_Logo.png/125px-HBO_2_Logo.png" decoding="async" width="125" height="94" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8b/HBO_2_Logo.png 1.5x" data-file-width="180" data-file-height="135" /></a><br /><b>HBO2</b></td>
<td style="text-align:left;">A secondary channel that features a separate schedule of theatrical and original made-for-cable movies, series and specials, as well as same-week rebroadcasts of newer films, boxing events and episodes of HBO original series aired recently on the primary channel. Unlike the main HBO channel, HBO2 broadcasts R-rated films during the daytime hours, along with its other channels (excluding HBO Family). Launched on August 1, 1991, the channel was renamed <b>HBO Plus</b> on April 1, 1998, but reverted to the original "HBO2" name in September 2002. In Latin America, a regional version of HBO2 rebroadcasts movies previously aired on the main <a href="/wiki/HBO_Latin_America_Group" title="HBO Latin America Group">HBO Latin America</a> channel, and HBO Plus functions as a separate channel.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:center; width:130pt;"><a href="/wiki/File:HBO_Comedy_Logo.png" class="image"><img alt="HBO Comedy Logo.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/HBO_Comedy_Logo.png/125px-HBO_Comedy_Logo.png" decoding="async" width="125" height="94" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/42/HBO_Comedy_Logo.png 1.5x" data-file-width="180" data-file-height="135" /></a><br /><b>HBO Comedy</b></td>
<td style="text-align:left;">Launched on May 6, 1999,<sup id="cite_ref-HBO_Laughs_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBO_Laughs-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> HBO Comedy features comedic films, as well as rebroadcasts of HBO's original comedy series and stand-up specials; the channel broadcasts R-rated films during the daytime hours, but only airs adult comedy specials at night.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:center; width:130pt;"><a href="/wiki/File:HBO_Family_Logo.png" class="image"><img alt="HBO Family Logo.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/HBO_Family_Logo.png/125px-HBO_Family_Logo.png" decoding="async" width="125" height="94" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a1/HBO_Family_Logo.png 1.5x" data-file-width="180" data-file-height="135" /></a><br /><b>HBO Family</b></td>
<td style="text-align:left;">Launched in December 1996,<sup id="cite_ref-HBO_Family_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBO_Family-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> HBO Family features movies and series aimed at children, as well as feature films intended for a broader family audience. It airs a <a href="/wiki/Block_programming" title="Block programming">block</a> of series aimed at preschoolers, "<a href="/wiki/HBO_Kids" title="HBO Kids">HBO Kids</a>" (formerly "Jam"), each morning from 6:00 to 11:00 a.m. and weekday afternoons from 4:00 to 5:00 pm. Eastern and Pacific Time; films and family-oriented original specials fill out the remainder of the channel's daily schedule.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> All films broadcast on HBO Family are rated G, PG or PG-13 (or the <a href="/wiki/TV_Parental_Guidelines" title="TV Parental Guidelines">equivalent</a> TV-G, TV-PG or TV-14); R or NC-17 rated films and TV-MA rated programs are not broadcast on the channel. Children's programs that formerly ran on the main HBO channel in the form of a daily morning block, with specials airing during the late afternoon/early evening hours; these programs migrated entirely to HBO Family by the early 2000s. With the exception of 1 hour at 9:00 AM and 9:30 AM Est of <a href="/wiki/Sesame_Street" title="Sesame Street">Sesame Street</a>, <a href="/wiki/Esme_%26_Roy" title="Esme &amp; Roy">Esme &amp; Roy</a> and an unbrand block of one to two movies appropriate for family viewing on Saturdays (before and after Sesame Street and Esme &amp; Roy), HBO does not air any ofter children's programs on its main channel any more. HBO Family is HBO's third (and only successful) venture at a family-oriented pay service: two similarly formatted standalone mini-pay services that were launched by the network, Take 2 in 1979 and Festival in 1987, both ceased operations after short existences. Despite being a premium service, cable providers have occasionally used HBO Family to temporarily replace television stations that were dropped due to <a href="/wiki/Carriage_dispute" title="Carriage dispute">carriage disputes</a> with providers such as during <a href="/wiki/Hearst_Television" title="Hearst Television">Hearst Television</a>'s 2012 dispute with Time Warner Cable that resulted in TWC's associated <a href="/wiki/Bright_House_Networks" title="Bright House Networks">Bright House Networks</a> system substituting independent station <a href="/wiki/WMOR-TV" title="WMOR-TV">WMOR-TV</a> with the channel in <a href="/wiki/Tampa,_Florida" title="Tampa, Florida">Tampa, Florida</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> and a dispute between <a href="/wiki/Cox_Communications" title="Cox Communications">Cox Communications</a> and <a href="/wiki/LIN_Media" title="LIN Media">LIN TV</a> in which HBO Family temporarily replaced Fox affiliate <a href="/wiki/WVBT" title="WVBT">WVBT</a> from Cox's <a href="/wiki/Hampton_Roads,_Virginia" class="mw-redirect" title="Hampton Roads, Virginia">Hampton Roads, Virginia</a> system from January to February 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:center; width:130pt;"><a href="/wiki/File:HBO_Latino_Logo.png" class="image"><img alt="HBO Latino Logo.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/HBO_Latino_Logo.png/125px-HBO_Latino_Logo.png" decoding="async" width="125" height="94" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/48/HBO_Latino_Logo.png 1.5x" data-file-width="180" data-file-height="135" /></a><br /><b>HBO Latino</b></td>
<td style="text-align:left;">Launched on October 31, 2000 (although originally slated to debut on September 18 of that year),<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> HBO Latino is a channel aimed at <a href="/wiki/Hispanic_and_Latino_Americans" title="Hispanic and Latino Americans">Hispanic and Latino American</a> audiences that largely serves as a Spanish language simulcast of the primary HBO channel, with the exception of some limited program substitutions and different network promotions featured in-between programs (HBO and its other multiplex channels also utilize the second audio program function included on many television sets, and cable and satellite receivers to provide alternate Spanish language audio tracks of most programs). The channel's programming includes HBO original productions, Spanish and Portuguese series from HBO Latin America, dubbed versions of Hollywood blockbusters, Spanish-language films and boxing events (including the original boxing series <i>Boxeo De Oro</i>). The channel is the successor to HBO en Español (originally named Selecciones en Español de HBO y Cinemax), which launched in 1989.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:center; width:130pt;"><a href="/wiki/File:HBO_Signature_Logo.png" class="image"><img alt="HBO Signature Logo.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/HBO_Signature_Logo.png/125px-HBO_Signature_Logo.png" decoding="async" width="125" height="94" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/75/HBO_Signature_Logo.png 1.5x" data-file-width="180" data-file-height="135" /></a><br /><b>HBO Signature</b></td>
<td style="text-align:left;">HBO Signature features high quality films, HBO original series and specials. Launched in 1991, the channel was originally known as "<b>HBO 3</b>" until October 1998, when its format was changed from a genericized format similar to HBO and HBO2 to focusing on movies, series and specials targeted at a female audience.<sup id="cite_ref-HBO_digital_networks_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBO_digital_networks-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:center; width:130pt;"><a href="/wiki/File:HBO_Zone_Logo.png" class="image"><img alt="HBO Zone Logo.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/HBO_Zone_Logo.png/125px-HBO_Zone_Logo.png" decoding="async" width="125" height="94" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fe/HBO_Zone_Logo.png 1.5x" data-file-width="180" data-file-height="135" /></a><br /><b>HBO Zone</b></td>
<td style="text-align:left;">Launched on May 6, 1999,<sup id="cite_ref-HBO_Laughs_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBO_Laughs-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> HBO Zone airs movies and HBO original programs aimed at young adults between the ages of 18 to 34 years old. It was also the only HBO channel that broadcasts <a href="/wiki/Softcore_pornography" title="Softcore pornography">softcore pornographic</a> programming, featuring adult-oriented movies similar to those seen on sister network Cinemax's <a href="/wiki/Max_After_Dark" class="mw-redirect" title="Max After Dark">Max After Dark</a> block on most days in late-night. Max After Dark was ceased in late 2013.
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cinemax">Cinemax</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Cinemax">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cinemax" title="Cinemax">Cinemax</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cinemax_(Yellow).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Cinemax_%28Yellow%29.svg/220px-Cinemax_%28Yellow%29.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="77" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Cinemax_%28Yellow%29.svg/330px-Cinemax_%28Yellow%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Cinemax_%28Yellow%29.svg/440px-Cinemax_%28Yellow%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="179" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cinemax_(Yellow).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cinemax logo</div></div></div>
<p>On August 1, 1980, HBO launched Cinemax, a companion movie-based premium channel created as HBO's answer to fellow movie-oriented pay service The Movie Channel (which operated as a standalone service at the time).<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Unlike HBO, Cinemax maintained a 24-hour schedule from its launch. The channel succeeded early on partly due to its reliance on movie classics from the 1950s to the 1970s—with some more recent films mixed in—that would be presented uncut and without commercial interruption, at a time when cable subscribers only received about three dozen channels due to limited <a href="/wiki/Cable_television_headend" title="Cable television headend">headend</a> channel capacity. In most cases, cable operators sold Cinemax and HBO as a single package, usually offered at a discount for customers that chose to subscribe to both channels.
