</p><p>In its early years, Cinemax carried music specials and some limited original programming such as <i><a href="/wiki/Second_City_Television" title="Second City Television">Second City Television</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Max_Headroom_(TV_series)" title="Max Headroom (TV series)">Max Headroom</a></i> in addition to movies, but the network subsequently become known among its subscribers for airing <a href="/wiki/Softcore_pornography" title="Softcore pornography">softcore adult films and series</a> during the late night hours that contain <a href="/wiki/Sexual_intercourse" title="Sexual intercourse">strong sexual content</a> and nudity (broadcasts of such programs are restricted from airing on the main Cinemax channel before 11:30 pm. Eastern Time),<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Skinemax_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Skinemax-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> and eventually began producing original action series in August 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-Skinemax_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Skinemax-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Beginning with the launch of Cinemax 2 (now MoreMax) in 1991, Cinemax has gradually launched its own set of multiplex services; as of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, in addition to its main feed and MoreMax, Cinemax operates five additional channels: ActionMax (which originally launched as Cinemax 3 in 1995); ThrillerMax (which launched in 1998);<sup id="cite_ref-HBO_digital_networks_77-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBO_digital_networks-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> MovieMax (which originally launched as WMax in 2001); Cinemáx (a Spanish language simulcast feed, which originally launched as the separately formatted @Max in 2001) and 5StarMax (which originally launched in 2001).<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_services">Other services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Other services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="HBO_HD">HBO HD</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: HBO HD">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>HBO HD is a <a href="/wiki/High-definition_television" title="High-definition television">high definition</a> simulcast feed of HBO that broadcasts in the <a href="/wiki/1080i" title="1080i">1080i</a> resolution format.<sup id="cite_ref-hbohd_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbohd-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> In addition to its main channel, HBO also operates high definition simulcast feeds of its six multiplex channels. HBO HD is available on most major cable providers including Cox Communications; <a href="/wiki/DirecTV" title="DirecTV">DirecTV</a>; <a href="/wiki/Charter_Communications" title="Charter Communications">Charter Communications</a>; Time Warner Cable; <a href="/wiki/Dish_Network" title="Dish Network">Dish Network</a>; <a href="/wiki/Comcast" title="Comcast">Comcast</a> (In 2016 Comcast confirmed that it was changing 1080i channels to 720p60);<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/AT%26T_U-verse" title="AT&amp;T U-verse">AT&amp;T U-verse</a>; <a href="/wiki/Optimum" class="mw-redirect" title="Optimum">Optimum</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Verizon_FiOS" class="mw-redirect" title="Verizon FiOS">Verizon FiOS</a>, although few providers offer all seven multiplex channels in HD. The main channel first began broadcasting in high definition on March 6, 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-HBO_HD_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBO_HD-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="HBO_on_Demand">HBO on Demand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: HBO on Demand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>HBO on Demand is the channel's <a href="/wiki/Video_on_demand" title="Video on demand">subscription video-on-demand</a> service; launched on July 1, 2001 on Time Warner Cable's Columbia, South Carolina system, it was the first subscription VOD service offered by a premium channel in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-HBO_On_Demand_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBO_On_Demand-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> HBO on Demand offers a selection of movies, original series and specials previously seen on the network.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> The service is provided at no additional cost to HBO subscribers, who already regularly pay a premium fee to cable and satellite providers to receive access to the channel. HBO launched the VOD service in an effort to allow subscribers access to the channel's programming on their own schedules, thereby reducing the frequency in which viewers were unable to find a program they would like to watch as well as limiting cancellations to the service for that same reason. HBO on Demand features a rotating selection of films, specials and series, with new titles added each Friday alongside existing program titles held over from the previous one to two weeks.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The standard definition and high definition versions of the HBO on Demand service are available on most cable and satellite providers, delivered to customers who subscribe to the linear HBO channels at no additional charge. On January 3, 2011, HBO became the first premium channel and the first cable network to offer a <a href="/wiki/3DTV" class="mw-redirect" title="3DTV">3D</a>-only VOD service as it launched a subscription video on demand service offering feature films in 3D to Time Warner Cable, Comcast and Verizon FiOS customers who subscribe to the HBO service.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United Kingdom, a domestic version of HBO on Demand launched on <a href="/wiki/TalkTalk_TV" title="TalkTalk TV">TalkTalk TV</a> in 2015, available through the provider's box sets as a strictly buy-and-keep service.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="HBO_Go">HBO Go</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: HBO Go">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/HBO_Go" title="HBO Go">HBO Go</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HBOGO.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/HBOGO.svg/220px-HBOGO.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="49" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/HBOGO.svg/330px-HBOGO.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/HBOGO.svg/440px-HBOGO.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="134" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HBOGO.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>HBO Go logo</div></div></div>
<p>On February 18, 2010, HBO launched HBO Go, a service that carries 1,000 hours of program content available for streaming in standard or high definition, intended as a <a href="/wiki/TV_Everywhere" title="TV Everywhere">TV Everywhere</a> service available only to existing subscribers of the linear HBO television channels (a requirement necessary to access its content via streaming devices such as <a href="/wiki/Roku" title="Roku">Roku</a> and <a href="/wiki/Apple_TV" title="Apple TV">Apple TV</a>, and some <a href="/wiki/Video_game_console" title="Video game console">video game consoles</a>, as well as via its website and mobile apps). Content available on HBO Go includes theatrically released films as well as HBO original programs, movies, comedy specials, documentaries, sports and late night adult programming.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="HBO_Now">HBO Now</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: HBO Now">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/HBO_Now" title="HBO Now">HBO Now</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HBONOW-logo.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/HBONOW-logo.svg/220px-HBONOW-logo.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="39" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/HBONOW-logo.svg/330px-HBONOW-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/HBONOW-logo.svg/440px-HBONOW-logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="179" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HBONOW-logo.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>HBO Now logo</div></div></div>
<p>On October 15, 2014, HBO announced plans to launch an <a href="/wiki/Over-the-top_content" class="mw-redirect" title="Over-the-top content">over-the-top</a> subscription video on demand service in 2015, which would be distributed as a standalone offering that does not require an existing television subscription to use.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-deadline-hbogoitalone_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deadline-hbogoitalone-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-atlantic-goitalone_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlantic-goitalone-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> The service, HBO Now, was unveiled on March 9, 2015, and officially launched one month later on April 7.<sup id="cite_ref-gigaom-hbonow_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gigaom-hbonow-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-verge-hbonow_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-verge-hbonow-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-verge-hbonow-launched_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-verge-hbonow-launched-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hbonow_launch_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbonow_launch-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> The service was initially available via <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple Inc.</a> to <a href="/wiki/Apple_TV" title="Apple TV">Apple TV</a> and <a href="/wiki/IOS" title="IOS">iOS</a> devices for a three-month exclusivity period following its formal launch, before becoming available for subscription through other participating <a href="/wiki/Internet_service_provider" title="Internet service provider">Internet service providers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-gigaom-hbonow_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gigaom-hbonow-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-verge-hbonow_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-verge-hbonow-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Available for $15 per month, HBO Now is identical to HBO Go in terms of content and features. New episodes of HBO series are made available for streaming the same day, and usually at the same time, as their original broadcast on the main linear HBO channel. Apple's <a href="/wiki/App_Store_(iOS)" title="App Store (iOS)">App Store</a> features promotions offering free one-month trials or other incentives to subscribe to HBO Now, as the program is in partnership with Apple Inc. and Apple TV.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The number of HBO Now subscribers reached over 8 million by February 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="HBO_Max">HBO Max</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: HBO Max">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/HBO_Max" title="HBO Max">HBO Max</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HBO_Max_Logo.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/HBO_Max_Logo.svg/220px-HBO_Max_Logo.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="38" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/HBO_Max_Logo.svg/330px-HBO_Max_Logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/HBO_Max_Logo.svg/440px-HBO_Max_Logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="104" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HBO_Max_Logo.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>HBO Max logo</div></div></div>
<p>On October 10, 2018, WarnerMedia announced that it will launch an <a href="/wiki/Over-the-top_media_services" class="mw-redirect" title="Over-the-top media services">over-the-top</a> <a href="/wiki/Streaming_media" title="Streaming media">streaming service</a> in late 2019, featuring content from its entertainment brands.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> WarnerMedia's parent <a href="/wiki/AT%26T" title="AT&amp;T">AT&amp;T</a> CEO Randall Stephenson indicated in mid-May 2019 that it would use the HBO brand and would tie into cable operators as HBO cable subscribers would have access to the streaming service. A beta was expected in the fourth quarter of 2019 and full launch in first quarter 2020 at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> On July 9, 2019, WarnerMedia announced the launch of their upcoming streaming service HBO Max would launch in spring 2020. Also announced at that time was that <i><a href="/wiki/Friends" title="Friends">Friends</a></i> would be taken off Netflix for the launch, while <a href="/wiki/Reese_Witherspoon" title="Reese Witherspoon">Reese Witherspoon</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Hello_Sunshine_(company)" title="Hello Sunshine (company)">Hello Sunshine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greg_Berlanti" title="Greg Berlanti">Greg Berlanti</a> were signed to production deals for the service.<sup id="cite_ref-vty_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vty-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Programming">Programming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Programming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_programs_broadcast_by_HBO" title="List of programs broadcast by HBO">List of programs broadcast by HBO</a></div>
<p>HBO's programming schedule currently consists largely of theatrically released feature films—which occupy the majority of its daily schedule—and original series primarily aimed at adults (including, as of November 2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, dramas such as <i><a href="/wiki/Game_of_Thrones" title="Game of Thrones">Game of Thrones</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Westworld_(TV_series)" title="Westworld (TV series)">Westworld</a></i>, and comedies such as <i><a href="/wiki/Insecure_(TV_series)" title="Insecure (TV series)">Insecure</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Veep" title="Veep">Veep</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Ballers" title="Ballers">Ballers</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Last_Week_Tonight_with_John_Oliver" title="Last Week Tonight with John Oliver">Last Week Tonight with John Oliver</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Silicon_Valley_(TV_series)" title="Silicon Valley (TV series)">Silicon Valley</a></i>). In addition, HBO also carries original made-for-TV movies, sports events and sports-centric documentary and magazine series, documentary films, behind-the-scenes specials, and concert and stand-up comedy specials. The network primarily airs most of its original programs on its main channel after 8:00 p.m. Eastern and Pacific Time, although it airs original series and made-for-cable movies as well as certain documentaries during the daytime hours; these programs also air at various times on HBO's multiplex channels. HBO Signature, HBO Family, HBO Comedy and HBO Zone also each carry archived HBO programming, airing repeats of former original series and specials dating back to the 1990s.
</p><p>HBO has long maintained a policy not to run R-rated films on its primary channel between 6:00 a.m. and 8:00 p.m. Eastern and Pacific Time;<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> this policy (which may have once stemmed from HBO's availability on analog cable tiers, while its multiplex channels generally required a <a href="/wiki/Digital_cable" title="Digital cable">digital cable</a> subscription or at least scrambling) remains in place as of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, despite the existence of the <a href="/wiki/V-chip" title="V-chip">V-chip</a> and other premium services having incorporated R-rated films onto their daytime schedules starting in the mid-1980s. The policy was also applied to all TV-MA rated programs after the <a href="/wiki/TV_Parental_Guidelines" title="TV Parental Guidelines">TV Parental Guidelines</a> were implemented on January 1, 1997; however the main HBO channel began airing a limited amount of TV-MA rated original series, movies and documentaries that contain some strong profanity and violence, but are largely devoid of nudity, and graphic violent or sexual content on weekends before 8:00 p.m. Eastern in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> HBO began occasionally rebroadcasting R-rated films as early as 4:00 p.m. Eastern Time in 2012, as part of its Sunday rebroadcast of the prior Saturday's movie premiere telecast, depending on the length of the film and the scheduling of any HBO original series that air after it. In 2017, the channel expanded its carriage of R-rated films to as early as 4:00 p.m. Eastern Time, regardless of the day but still on a periodic basis.
</p><p>Outside of HBO Family, which does not run any programs with either a TV-MA or R rating,<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> HBO's other multiplex channels will air TV-MA and <a href="/wiki/Motion_Picture_Association_of_America_film_rating_system" title="Motion Picture Association of America film rating system">R-rated</a> programming during morning and afternoon time periods. HBO also does not typically allow most NC-17 rated films to be aired on the primary channel or its multiplex channels.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>HBO pioneered the <a href="/wiki/Free_preview" title="Free preview">free preview</a> concept—which has since become a standard in the pay television industry—in 1973, as part of a plan to increase subscribership of the channel.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Cable providers were originally granted permission to carry HBO on a local origination channel in order for those who are not subscribers the ability to view the channel for a limited number of days; with the advent of digital cable and satellite, providers now unencrypt the designated slots of each HBO channel during preview periods. Until the mid-1990s, on-air promotions featured between programs were replaced (and later, merely interspersed) with interstitials featuring on-air hosts asking viewers to subscribe to the service. Although participation was voluntary,<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> preview events are carried by most major and some smaller pay television providers (the number of providers and the providers that choose to offer the event varies depending on the given free preview period, and may not be carried on all systems owned by a <a href="/wiki/Multiple_system_operator" class="mw-redirect" title="Multiple system operator">multiple system operator</a> unless at the provider's discretion); HBO currently offers between three and five preview events each year to participating providers (which are normally scheduled to coincide with the premiere of a new or returning original series, and in the past, a high-profile special or feature film).
</p><p>The network also produces short segments promoting new movies with the cooperation of the film studios that hold releasing rights to the projects. These usually consist of either interstitial segments providing a behind-the-scenes look at the making of an upcoming/recently released film, with interviews with the actors and principal crew, or <a href="/wiki/Red_carpet" title="Red carpet">red carpet</a> coverage, which are almost universally produced by studios with which HBO and Cinemax maintain exclusive premium television broadcast rights. Depending on their length or content, these are either aired as part of the feature segment <i>HBO News</i> (formerly titled <i>HBO Entertainment News</i> from 1988 to 2007), which airs during extended promotional breaks between programs and runs between three and five minutes, or as part of <i>HBO First Look</i>, a series of documentary-style interstitial specials (usually running 15 to 20 minutes in length, with no set schedule) that debuted in 1992. These segments, particularly episodes of <i>First Look</i>, have also often been included as bonus features on DVD and <a href="/wiki/Blu-ray_Disc" class="mw-redirect" title="Blu-ray Disc">Blu-ray</a> releases of the films that were profiled (many of which have aired on HBO and Cinemax once they reached their pay-cable distribution windows), though broadcasts of these interstitials have begun to be reduced to only a few episodes per year as HBO has focused on its higher-profile, long-form original programming instead and studios have internally produced behind-the-scenes featurettes for their films for exclusive physical and digital media release.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>During the earlier years of the network, various short films would be aired in-between films and other programming, originally billed as <i>Something Short and Special</i>. Around 1980, <i>InterMissions</i>, as they were now called, were bannered in two groupings: <i><a href="/wiki/Video_Jukebox_(TV_series)" title="Video Jukebox (TV series)">Video Jukebox</a></i>, for when music videos were played (these segments were eventually separated from the other intermission shorts and gained various longform spinoffs, also titled as <i>Video Jukebox</i> or variants thereof), and <i>Special</i>, for the various short films. By 1984, the shorts had largely been reduced to comedic shorts (originally named <i>HBO Comedy Shorts</i> and then as <i>HBO Short Takes</i>, which used a set of different animated intros) and <i>HBO Shorts for Kids</i>, seen largely before and during family-oriented programming. By the end of the decade, intermission shorts had largely vanished from the service.
</p><p>During the "Executive Actions" symposium held by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_University" title="George Washington University">George Washington University</a> in April 2015 (shortly after the launch of the HBO Now streaming service), HBO CEO <a href="/wiki/Richard_Plepler" title="Richard Plepler">Richard Plepler</a> said that he does not want the network to be akin to <a href="/wiki/Netflix" title="Netflix">Netflix</a> in which users "<a href="/wiki/Binge_watching" class="mw-redirect" title="Binge watching">binge watch</a>" its television shows and film content, saying "I don't think it would have been a great thing for HBO or our brand if that had been gobbled up in the first week[...] I think it was very exciting for the viewer to have that mystery held out for an extended period of time." Pleper cited that he feels that binge watching does not correlate with the culture of HBO and HBO watchers.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Original_programming">Original programming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Original programming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_programs_broadcast_by_HBO" title="List of programs broadcast by HBO">List of programs broadcast by HBO</a></div>
<p>Since the early 1980s, HBO has produced original programming, which include dramatic and comedic series, in addition to its slate of theatrical films. Most of these shows are intended for adults (and, with limited exceptions, are typically assigned <a href="/wiki/TV_Parental_Guidelines#TV-MA" title="TV Parental Guidelines">TV-MA ratings</a>), often featuring high amounts of profanity, violence, sexual themes and/or nudity that would be much more difficult to get on <a href="/wiki/Basic_cable" class="mw-redirect" title="Basic cable">basic cable</a> or over-the-air broadcast channels, out of fear of losing sponsors. However some of its original programs, primarily those produced before 2001, have also been aimed at families or children; most of these type of programs have migrated to HBO Family, though HBO has produced very few newer family-oriented series for either channel since that point. In a notable example, HBO ventured back into children's programming with its acquisition of the first-run and streaming rights to <i>Sesame Street</i>, a long-running children's television series that had previously aired on PBS for the vast majority of its run, in the aforementioned deal with Sesame Workshop that was announced in August 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-cnnmoney-sesamestreet_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnnmoney-sesamestreet-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to maintaining rights to films from various distributors, HBO also produces its own made-for-cable movies through <a href="/wiki/HBO_Films" title="HBO Films">HBO Films</a>; the film division, originally named HBO Premiere Films (then, HBO Pictures), began producing original movies for the network in 1983 with the debut of <i>The Terry Fox Story</i>. Unlike most television films produced for cable television, most of the original movies produced by HBO have featured major film actors over the years, ranging from <a href="/wiki/James_Stewart" title="James Stewart">James Stewart</a> to <a href="/wiki/Michael_Douglas" title="Michael Douglas">Michael Douglas</a>. The channel also produces stand-up comedy specials, which were formerly broadcast under the <i><a href="/wiki/On_Location_(TV_series)" title="On Location (TV series)">On Location</a></i>, <i>HBO Comedy Hour</i> and <i>HBO Comedy Half-Hour</i> banners, which periodically premiere on certain Saturday nights when a boxing match or movie is not scheduled during the late prime time slots.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>One of HBO's first successful specials was <i>The Bette Midler Show</i> in 1976, which launched the <i><a href="/wiki/Standing_Room_Only_(TV_series)" title="Standing Room Only (TV series)">Standing Room Only</a></i> concert series. For a time in the early 1980s, HBO produced a concert special almost every other month, featuring major music stars such as <a href="/wiki/Boy_George" title="Boy George">Boy George</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Who" title="The Who">The Who</a>. After <a href="/wiki/MTV" title="MTV">MTV</a>'s successful rollout in 1981, the <i>Standing Room Only</i> series began to produce fewer concerts, but focused more on "world class" music events featuring artists such as <a href="/wiki/Elton_John" title="Elton John">Elton John</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tina_Turner" title="Tina Turner">Tina Turner</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barbra_Streisand" title="Barbra Streisand">Barbra Streisand</a>, as well as fundraisers such as <a href="/wiki/Farm_Aid" title="Farm Aid">Farm Aid</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-An_Original_Voice_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-An_Original_Voice-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The <i>On Location</i> comedy specials, which presented a stand-up comedian's performance in its entirety and uncut, began in 1975 with a special starring <a href="/wiki/Robert_Klein" title="Robert Klein">Robert Klein</a>. The first of twelve concert specials televised by the network featuring <a href="/wiki/George_Carlin" title="George Carlin">George Carlin</a> aired on HBO in 1977 as part of <i>On Location</i>, featuring Carlin's first televised performance of his classic routine, "<a href="/wiki/Seven_dirty_words" title="Seven dirty words">The Seven Words You Can Never Say on Television</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-An_Original_Voice_30-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-An_Original_Voice-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> As other cable channels incorporated comedy specials due to their inexpensive format, HBO began to model its strategy with its comedy specials after its music programming, focusing on a few specials each year featuring popular comedians.<sup id="cite_ref-An_Original_Voice_30-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-An_Original_Voice-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Movie_library">Movie library</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Movie library">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As of May 2016,<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> HBO, as well as its sister channel Cinemax, maintains exclusive first-run film licensing agreements with network sister company <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros." class="mw-redirect" title="Warner Bros.">Warner Bros.</a> (including content from subsidiaries <a href="/wiki/Warner_Animation_Group" title="Warner Animation Group">Warner Animation Group</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Line_Cinema" title="New Line Cinema">New Line Cinema</a> since 2005, and <a href="/wiki/Castle_Rock_Entertainment" title="Castle Rock Entertainment">Castle Rock Entertainment</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/20th_Century_Fox" class="mw-redirect" title="20th Century Fox">20th Century Fox</a> since 1979 (including subsidiaries <a href="/wiki/20th_Century_Fox_Animation" title="20th Century Fox Animation">20th Century Fox Animation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blue_Sky_Studios" title="Blue Sky Studios">Blue Sky Studios</a>, <a href="/wiki/Regency_Enterprises" title="Regency Enterprises">New Regency Productions</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fox_Searchlight_Pictures" class="mw-redirect" title="Fox Searchlight Pictures">Fox Searchlight Pictures</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Universal_Pictures" title="Universal Pictures">Universal Pictures</a> since 2003 (including content from subsidiaries <a href="/wiki/Universal_Animation_Studios" title="Universal Animation Studios">Universal Animation Studios</a>, <a href="/wiki/DreamWorks_Animation" title="DreamWorks Animation">DreamWorks Animation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Working_Title_Films" title="Working Title Films">Working Title Films</a>, <a href="/wiki/Savoy_Pictures" title="Savoy Pictures">Savoy Pictures</a>, <a href="/wiki/Illumination_Entertainment" class="mw-redirect" title="Illumination Entertainment">Illumination Entertainment</a> and <a href="/wiki/Focus_Features" title="Focus Features">Focus Features</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Summit_Entertainment" title="Summit Entertainment">Summit Entertainment</a> since June 1, 2013,<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/DreamWorks_Pictures" title="DreamWorks Pictures">DreamWorks</a> since 1996 (excluding films that DreamWorks co-produces in conjunction with <a href="/wiki/Touchstone_Pictures" title="Touchstone Pictures">Touchstone Pictures</a>, with rights to live action co-productions by the two studios being held by Showtime).<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first-run film output agreement with Fox was renewed by HBO for ten years on August 15, 2012 (with a provision allowing the studio to release its films through digital platforms such as <a href="/wiki/ITunes" title="ITunes">iTunes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amazon.com" class="mw-redirect" title="Amazon.com">Amazon</a> during a film's term of license with the channel for the first time),<sup id="cite_ref-HBO_Fox_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBO_Fox-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> and the Universal output deal was renewed for ten years on January 6, 2013 (with the exception of certain animated films that HBO can offer to pass over to the <a href="/wiki/Netflix" title="Netflix">Netflix</a> streaming service).<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> The first-run output deal with Summit Entertainment was renewed by HBO for an additional four years on March 1, 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-HBO_Chief_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBO_Chief-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> Since 2008, HBO also holds exclusive pay cable rights to its own in-house theatrical films made through HBO Films. While <a href="/wiki/The_Walt_Disney_Studios_(production_company)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Walt Disney Studios (production company)">Walt Disney Studios Motion Pictures</a> fully acquired 20th Century Fox in March 2019, Disney has an output deal with its own <a href="/wiki/Disney%2B" title="Disney+">Disney+</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hulu" title="Hulu">Hulu</a> services, whereas Fox's contract with HBO expires in 2022.<sup id="cite_ref-HBO_Fox_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBO_Fox-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>HBO also shows sub-runs—runs of films that have already received broadcast or syndicated television airings—of theatrical films from <a href="/wiki/Paramount_Pictures" title="Paramount Pictures">Paramount Pictures</a> (including content from subsidiaries <a href="/wiki/The_Cannon_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="The Cannon Group">The Cannon Group</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carolco_Pictures" title="Carolco Pictures">Carolco Pictures</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nickelodeon_Movies" title="Nickelodeon Movies">Nickelodeon Movies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Republic_Pictures" title="Republic Pictures">Republic Pictures</a>, all for films released prior to 2006), <a href="/wiki/The_Walt_Disney_Studios_(production_company)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Walt Disney Studios (production company)">Walt Disney Studios Motion Pictures</a> (including content from subsidiaries <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Pictures" title="Walt Disney Pictures">Walt Disney Pictures</a>, <a href="/wiki/Touchstone_Pictures" title="Touchstone Pictures">Touchstone Pictures</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hollywood_Pictures" title="Hollywood Pictures">Hollywood Pictures</a>, and formerly owned subsidiary <a href="/wiki/Miramax" title="Miramax">Miramax</a>), <a href="/wiki/Sony_Pictures_Entertainment" class="mw-redirect" title="Sony Pictures Entertainment">Sony Pictures Entertainment</a> (including content from subsidiaries <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Pictures" title="Columbia Pictures">Columbia Pictures</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sony_Pictures_Classics" title="Sony Pictures Classics">Sony Pictures Classics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Embassy_Pictures" title="Embassy Pictures">Embassy Pictures</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Creek_Entertainment_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="Morgan Creek Entertainment Group">Morgan Creek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Screen_Gems" title="Screen Gems">Screen Gems</a> and former HBO sister company <a href="/wiki/TriStar_Pictures" title="TriStar Pictures">TriStar Pictures</a>, all for films released prior to 2005), <a href="/wiki/Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer" title="Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer">Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer</a> (including content from subsidiaries <a href="/wiki/United_Artists" title="United Artists">United Artists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orion_Pictures" title="Orion Pictures">Orion Pictures</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Samuel_Goldwyn_Company" title="The Samuel Goldwyn Company">The Samuel Goldwyn Company</a>), <a href="/wiki/Lions_Gate_Entertainment" class="mw-redirect" title="Lions Gate Entertainment">Lions Gate Entertainment</a> (for films released prior to 2009),<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/New_World_Pictures" title="New World Pictures">New World Pictures</a>.
</p><p>Films to which HBO holds the pay cable rights will usually also run on Cinemax during their licensing term, although some feature films from the aforementioned studios that the two channels have broadcast rights to will make their premium television debut on HBO several weeks before their premiere on <a href="/wiki/Cinemax" title="Cinemax">Cinemax</a> and vice versa.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Former_first-run_contracts">Former first-run contracts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Former first-run contracts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>During the early years of premium cable, it was not uncommon for multiple pay television services, including HBO, Showtime and The Movie Channel (and later, Cinemax), to hold broadcast rights to the same feature films. HBO began purchasing exclusive rights to broadcast individual films in the late 1970s; these gradually expanded to exclusive output deals (which are commonplace with North American premium channels to this day), in which a pay service enters into a licensing agreement to broadcast movies from a particular film studio over a period of years. HBO signed its first major exclusive film output deal with Columbia Pictures in the early 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-Movie_Duels_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Movie_Duels-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> During the 1980s, HBO also held rights to films from TriStar Pictures (whose output deal with HBO, as well as that with Columbia Pictures, expired after 2004), <a href="/wiki/New_World_Pictures" title="New World Pictures">New World Pictures</a> and Orion Pictures;<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> as of February&#160;2013<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, rival premium channel <a href="/wiki/Starz_(TV_channel)" class="mw-redirect" title="Starz (TV channel)">Starz</a> has an exclusive deal with Sony Pictures Entertainment until 2021, and is the rightsholder to all newer films from Columbia and TriStar.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Films released by Paramount Pictures between mid-1988 and late 1997 were broadcast on HBO;<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> rival premium channel <a href="/wiki/Showtime_(TV_network)" title="Showtime (TV network)">Showtime</a> assumed pay television rights to Paramount-released films in 1998, and held them until 2008, with the rights being turned over to upstart pay service <a href="/wiki/Epix_(TV_network)" class="mw-redirect" title="Epix (TV network)">Epix</a> (which Paramount and its corporate parent <a href="/wiki/Viacom_(2005%E2%80%93present)" class="mw-redirect" title="Viacom (2005–present)">Viacom</a> had partially owned) the following year.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> HBO relinquished its deal with DreamWorks Pictures to broadcast its live-action films at the end of 2010, when the distribution rights shifted from Paramount Pictures to Touchstone Pictures (whose films are broadcast by Showtime through a distribution agreement with the <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Studios_(division)" title="Walt Disney Studios (division)">Walt Disney Motion Pictures Group</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> HBO's contract with <a href="/wiki/DreamWorks_Animation" title="DreamWorks Animation">DreamWorks Animation</a> expired after 2012, at which time <a href="/wiki/Netflix" title="Netflix">Netflix</a> assumed pay television rights to that studio's releases.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports_programming">Sports programming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Sports programming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>HBO broadcasts a limited amount of sports programming as well as sports-related discussion and documentary series produced by the channel's HBO Sports division; HBO—through its parent holding company Home Box Office Inc.—also operates HBO PPV (formerly TVKO), which serves as a distributor of major <a href="/wiki/Boxing" title="Boxing">boxing</a> events for <a href="/wiki/Pay-per-view" title="Pay-per-view">pay-per-view</a>.
</p><p>HBO's first sports broadcast was of a New York Rangers-Vancouver Canucks NHL game, transmitted to a Service Electric cable system in Wilkes-Barre, Pennsylvania on November 8, 1972; the channel continued to air NHL hockey games through the mid-1970s. HBO has long been known for its telecasts of boxing matches (which usually air on Saturday nights every two to three weeks on average), including those shown on its flagship sports program <i>HBO World Championship Boxing</i>. On September 30, 1975, the "Thrilla in Manila" boxing match between Muhammad Ali and Joe Frazier aired on HBO and was the first program on the pay cable network to be broadcast via satellite. <a href="/wiki/1975_Wimbledon_Championships" title="1975 Wimbledon Championships">That same year</a>, HBO began airing coverage of <a href="/wiki/The_Championships,_Wimbledon" title="The Championships, Wimbledon">Wimbledon</a>; it held contractual rights to coverage of the tennis tournament through <a href="/wiki/1999_Wimbledon_Championships" title="1999 Wimbledon Championships">1999</a>, when it lost the rights to sister network <a href="/wiki/TNT_(U.S._TV_network)" class="mw-redirect" title="TNT (U.S. TV network)">TNT</a> (owned by Time Warner's <a href="/wiki/Turner_Broadcasting_System" title="Turner Broadcasting System">Turner Broadcasting System</a> subsidiary).
</p><p>In 1973, HBO aired a <a href="/wiki/World_Wrestling_Entertainment" class="mw-redirect" title="World Wrestling Entertainment">World Wide Wrestling Federation</a> event from <a href="/wiki/Madison_Square_Garden" title="Madison Square Garden">Madison Square Garden</a>, headlined by a match between <a href="/wiki/George_Steele" title="George Steele">George Steele</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pedro_Morales" title="Pedro Morales">Pedro Morales</a>. During the mid-1970s, HBO aired several basketball games from the <a href="/wiki/National_Basketball_Association" title="National Basketball Association">National Basketball Association</a> and the <a href="/wiki/American_Basketball_Association" title="American Basketball Association">American Basketball Association</a> (notably, <a href="/wiki/1976_ABA_Playoffs" title="1976 ABA Playoffs">the last ABA Finals game</a> in 1976, prior to the latter league's merger with the NBA, between the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Nets" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Nets">New York Nets</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Denver_Nuggets" title="Denver Nuggets">Denver Nuggets</a>). HBO also aired <a href="/wiki/Professional_Bowlers_Association" title="Professional Bowlers Association">Professional Bowlers Association</a> (PBA) events during the 1970s; <a href="/wiki/Dick_Stockton" title="Dick Stockton">Dick Stockton</a> served as the <a href="/wiki/Play-by-play" class="mw-redirect" title="Play-by-play">play-by-play</a> announcer and Skee Foremsky acted as the <a href="/wiki/Color_commentator" title="Color commentator">color commentator</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Bowling" title="Bowling">bowling</a> telecasts.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1977, HBO premiered the channel's longest-running program, and its first sports-related documentary and analysis series <i><a href="/wiki/Inside_the_NFL" title="Inside the NFL">Inside the NFL</a></i>, featuring game reviews of <a href="/wiki/National_Football_League" title="National Football League">National Football League</a> games from the previous week of the league season as well as interviews with players, coaches and team management; HBO canceled the program in February 2008 after 30 seasons (the program was later acquired by rival premium channel Showtime, which began airing the series in September 2008). HBO expanded its boxing slate in September 1996, with the launch of <i>Boxing After Dark</i>, a program which showcases fights from up-and-coming boxing talents. HBO eliminated its boxing programming in 2018, which by this point was the last live sports on the network, as by this point, network executives were of the opinion that "HBO (was) not a sports network."<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The network would build upon <i>Inside the NFL</i> with debut of additional sports talk and documentary programs: <i><a href="/wiki/Race_for_the_Pennant" title="Race for the Pennant">Race for the Pennant</a></i> (concerning the <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball" title="Major League Baseball">Major League Baseball</a> season, running from 1978 to 1992), <i><a href="/wiki/Real_Sports_with_Bryant_Gumbel" title="Real Sports with Bryant Gumbel">Real Sports with Bryant Gumbel</a></i> (which debuted in 1995), <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Record_with_Bob_Costas" title="On the Record with Bob Costas">On the Record with Bob Costas</a></i> (which debuted in 2001, and was revamped as <i><a href="/wiki/Costas_Now" title="Costas Now">Costas Now</a></i> in 2005, ending in 2009), and <i><a href="/wiki/Joe_Buck_Live" title="Joe Buck Live">Joe Buck Live</a></i> (which ran for one season in 2009). In 2001, HBO and <a href="/wiki/NFL_Films" title="NFL Films">NFL Films</a> began to jointly produce the documentary series <i><a href="/wiki/Hard_Knocks_(TV_series)" title="Hard Knocks (TV series)">Hard Knocks</a></i>, which follows an individual <a href="/wiki/National_Football_League" title="National Football League">NFL</a> team each season during <a href="/wiki/Training_camp" title="Training camp">training camp</a> and their preparations for the upcoming football season.<sup id="cite_ref-An_Original_Voice_30-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-An_Original_Voice-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>HBO Sports has been headed by several well-known television executives over the years, including its founder Steve Powell (later head of programming at <a href="/wiki/ESPN" title="ESPN">ESPN</a>), Dave Meister (later head of the <a href="/wiki/Tennis_Channel" title="Tennis Channel">Tennis Channel</a>), Seth Abraham (later head of <a href="/wiki/MSG_(TV_network)" class="mw-redirect" title="MSG (TV network)">MSG Network</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-An_Original_Voice_30-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-An_Original_Voice-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Ross_Greenburg" title="Ross Greenburg">Ross Greenburg</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Documentaries">Documentaries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Documentaries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexandra_Pelosi_and_Jim_McGreevey_Fall_to_Grace.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Alexandra_Pelosi_and_Jim_McGreevey_Fall_to_Grace.JPG/220px-Alexandra_Pelosi_and_Jim_McGreevey_Fall_to_Grace.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Alexandra_Pelosi_and_Jim_McGreevey_Fall_to_Grace.JPG/330px-Alexandra_Pelosi_and_Jim_McGreevey_Fall_to_Grace.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Alexandra_Pelosi_and_Jim_McGreevey_Fall_to_Grace.JPG/440px-Alexandra_Pelosi_and_Jim_McGreevey_Fall_to_Grace.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2912" data-file-height="2128" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexandra_Pelosi_and_Jim_McGreevey_Fall_to_Grace.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Filmmaker <a href="/wiki/Alexandra_Pelosi" title="Alexandra Pelosi">Alexandra Pelosi</a> and former <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_New_Jersey" title="Governor of New Jersey">New Jersey Governor</a> <a href="/wiki/Jim_McGreevey" title="Jim McGreevey">Jim McGreevey</a> at the New York City premiere of Pelosi's HBO documentary about McGreevey, <i><a href="/wiki/Fall_to_Grace_(documentary)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fall to Grace (documentary)">Fall to Grace</a></i>, in March 2013.</div></div></div>
<p>Many of HBO's documentary series appear under the <i><a href="/wiki/America_Undercover" title="America Undercover">America Undercover</a></i> brand, the regular features of which have been <i><a href="/wiki/Real_Sex" title="Real Sex">Real Sex</a></i> (a late night magazine-formatted series of specials that ran from 1992 to 2009, which frankly explored a variety of mainstream and non-mainstream sexual matters<sup id="cite_ref-An_Original_Voice_30-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-An_Original_Voice-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>) and <i><a href="/wiki/Autopsy_(TV_series)" title="Autopsy (TV series)">Autopsy</a></i>. One of the most notable <i>America Undercover</i> specials was 1985's <i>Soldiers in Hiding</i>, focusing on <a href="/wiki/Homelessness" title="Homelessness">homeless</a> veterans of the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a> living in the wilderness, which won the first <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="Academy Award">Academy Award</a> for a cable television service in the Best Documentary category (although HBO has had some of its documentaries enter limited theatrical release to qualify for Oscar nominations in later years).<sup id="cite_ref-An_Original_Voice_30-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-An_Original_Voice-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> HBO is also noted for its <i>Sports of the 20th Century</i> documentary brand. One of its most recent documentaries was <i>Dare to Dream</i>, about the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Women%27s_Soccer_Team" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Women&#39;s Soccer Team">U.S. Women's Soccer Team</a> and their effort to make a difference, and featured <a href="/wiki/Mia_Hamm" title="Mia Hamm">Mia Hamm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kristine_Lilly" title="Kristine Lilly">Kristine Lilly</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brandi_Chastain" title="Brandi Chastain">Brandi Chastain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joy_Fawcett" title="Joy Fawcett">Joy Fawcett</a> and <a href="/wiki/Julie_Foudy" title="Julie Foudy">Julie Foudy</a>.
</p><p>HBO's first successful documentary aired in 1979, the six-part series <i><a href="/wiki/Time_Was" title="Time Was">Time Was</a></i>, which featured host <a href="/wiki/Dick_Cavett" title="Dick Cavett">Dick Cavett</a> being inserted into seminal events occurring between the 1920s and the 1970s. 1981's <i>She's Nobody's Baby</i>, produced by <i><a href="/wiki/Ms._(magazine)" title="Ms. (magazine)">Ms.</a></i> magazine, was another well-known documentary tracing the evolution in the societal role of American women during the 20th Century; the special earned HBO the first <a href="/wiki/Peabody_Award" title="Peabody Award">Peabody Award</a> won by a pay television service.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> Since then, the network has brought home numerous Peabody Awards for its documentary films.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> HBO had also broadcast informational documentaries produced in partnership with <i><a href="/wiki/Consumer_Reports" title="Consumer Reports">Consumer Reports</a></i> starting in 1980, focusing on subjects from product safety to finance to health.<sup id="cite_ref-An_Original_Voice_30-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-An_Original_Voice-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> One such documentary, <i>AIDS: Everything You and Your Family Need to Know...But Were Afraid to Ask</i>, which aired in 1987 at the height of the AIDS epidemic in the U.S., provided factual information on <a href="/wiki/HIV-AIDS" class="mw-redirect" title="HIV-AIDS">AIDS and HIV</a> and was hosted by then-<a href="/wiki/Surgeon_General_of_the_United_States" title="Surgeon General of the United States">Surgeon General</a> <a href="/wiki/C._Everett_Koop" title="C. Everett Koop">C. Everett Koop</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-An_Original_Voice_30-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-An_Original_Voice-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2004, guided by human rights activist <a href="/wiki/Ansar_Burney" title="Ansar Burney">Ansar Burney</a>, an HBO team for <i>Real Sports with Bryant Gumbel</i> used a hidden camera to document slavery and torture in secret desert camps where boys under the age of five were trained to <a href="/wiki/Camel_racing" title="Camel racing">race camels</a>, a national sport in the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a> (UAE). This half-hour investigative report exposed a carefully hidden <a href="/wiki/Child_slavery" title="Child slavery">child slavery</a> ring that bought or <a href="/wiki/Kidnapping" title="Kidnapping">kidnapped</a> hundreds of young boys in Pakistan and Bangladesh, who were then forced to become camel jockeys in the UAE. The report also questioned the sincerity of U.S. diplomacy in pressuring the UAE, an ally to the United States, to comply with its own stated policy of banning the use of children under 15 from camel racing. The documentary won a Sports Emmy Award in 2004 for "Outstanding Sports Journalism" and the 2006 <a href="/wiki/Alfred_I._duPont%E2%80%93Columbia_University_Award" title="Alfred I. duPont–Columbia University Award">Alfred I. duPont–Columbia University Award</a> for "Outstanding Broadcast Journalism". It also brought worldwide attention to the plight of child camel jockeys in the Middle East and helped the Ansar Burney Trust to convince the governments of <a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar">Qatar</a> and the UAE to end the use of children in the sport.
</p><p>In 2006, film director <a href="/wiki/Spike_Lee" title="Spike Lee">Spike Lee</a> made a two-part four-hour documentary on <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Katrina" title="Hurricane Katrina">Hurricane Katrina</a> called <i><a href="/wiki/When_the_Levees_Broke:_A_Requiem_in_Four_Acts" class="mw-redirect" title="When the Levees Broke: A Requiem in Four Acts">When the Levees Broke: A Requiem in Four Acts</a></i>. Also in 2006, documentary artist <a href="/wiki/Lauren_Greenfield" title="Lauren Greenfield">Lauren Greenfield</a> directed a feature-length film about four young women struggling with <a href="/wiki/Eating_disorder" title="Eating disorder">eating disorders</a> seeking treatment at the Renfrew Clinic in Florida, called <i><a href="/wiki/Thin_(film)" title="Thin (film)">Thin</a></i>. 2008 saw the U.S. television premiere of <i><a href="/wiki/Baghdad_High" class="mw-redirect" title="Baghdad High">Baghdad High</a></i>, a documentary that depicted the lives of four boys attending a high school in <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, over the course of one year in the form of a video diary that was filmed by the boys themselves, who were given video cameras for the project.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In November 2008, HBO paid low seven figures for U.S. television rights to Amy Rice and Alicia Sams's documentary, <i>By the People: The Election of <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a></i>. The film covers Obama's 2006 trip to Africa, his presidential primary campaign, the <a href="/wiki/2008_United_States_presidential_election" title="2008 United States presidential election">2008 general election</a> and his <a href="/wiki/First_inauguration_of_Barack_Obama" title="First inauguration of Barack Obama">inauguration</a>. The documentary received theatrical release in New York City and Los Angeles, and aired on HBO in November 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In November 2012, HBO aired a four-part documentary titled <i>Witness</i>, each part of which is devoted to covering photojournalists in four conflict regions: <a href="/wiki/Ciudad_Ju%C3%A1rez" title="Ciudad Juárez">Juarez</a>, <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Rio de Janeiro">Rio de Janeiro</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> On March 28, 2013, the channel premiered the <a href="/wiki/Alexandra_Pelosi" title="Alexandra Pelosi">Alexandra Pelosi</a>-directed documentary <i><a href="/wiki/Fall_to_Grace_(documentary)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fall to Grace (documentary)">Fall to Grace</a></i>, about former <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_New_Jersey" title="Governor of New Jersey">New Jersey Governor</a> <a href="/wiki/Jim_McGreevey" title="Jim McGreevey">Jim McGreevey</a>, who resigned from the post in 2011 following the revelation of an infidelity scandal that led McGreevey to come out as <a href="/wiki/Homosexuality" title="Homosexuality">gay</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> On April 10, 2013, HBO aired <i>50 Children: The Rescue Mission of Mr. and Mrs. Kraus</i>, a documentary about the story of Gilbert and Eleanor Kraus, a Jewish couple from <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a> who traveled to <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> in 1939 and, with the help of the <a href="/wiki/B%27rith_Sholom" title="B&#39;rith Sholom">B'rith Sholom</a> fraternal organization, saved Jewish children in <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a> from likely death in <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">The Holocaust</a> by finding them new homes in Philadelphia. In April 2013, the channel aired the documentary <i><a href="/wiki/An_Apology_to_Elephants" title="An Apology to Elephants">An Apology to Elephants</a></i>, about the purported abuse and brutal treatment of elephants.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_ventures">Other ventures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Other ventures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In addition to its linear television channels, HBO has entered into other ventures:
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Television_channels">Television channels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Television channels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Take_2">Take 2</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Take 2">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In April 1979, HBO launched its first attempt at a spin-off service, Take 2. Designed as a family-oriented mini-pay service, Take 2 was essentially formatted as an alternative to HBO, without any R-rated program content. The channel was ultimately deemed a major failure due to low subscribership and limited carriage by cable providers, and ceased operations late that summer.<sup id="cite_ref-Movie_Duels_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Movie_Duels-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> HBO management analysed the mistakes that led to Take 2's downfall, which would result in the development of the network's second and more successful attempt at a secondary pay service, the movie-focused Cinemax, which launched on August 1, 1980.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Festival">Festival</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Festival">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hbosfestival.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e2/Hbosfestival.png/180px-Hbosfestival.png" decoding="async" width="180" height="68" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e2/Hbosfestival.png/270px-Hbosfestival.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/e/e2/Hbosfestival.png 2x" data-file-width="302" data-file-height="114" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hbosfestival.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Logo of HBO's early family-friendly service <i>Festival</i></div></div></div>	
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a href="/wiki/File:FestivalChannelguide0188.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/FestivalChannelguide0188.jpg/100px-FestivalChannelguide0188.jpg" decoding="async" width="100" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/FestivalChannelguide0188.jpg/150px-FestivalChannelguide0188.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/FestivalChannelguide0188.jpg/200px-FestivalChannelguide0188.jpg 2x" data-file-width="260" data-file-height="382" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FestivalChannelguide0188.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Example of <i>Festival's</i> monthly guide provided to subscribers (January 1988).</div></div></div>
<p>In the mid of 1986,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="Statement needs to be more specific about the content to which it refers. (May 2016)">specify</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> HBO launched the premium channel <b>Festival</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-Festival_program_guide_1987_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Festival_program_guide_1987-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> a separate service that was distinctively programmed to provide <a href="/wiki/Family-friendly" title="Family-friendly">family-friendly</a> fare, which featured <a href="/wiki/Golden_age_(metaphor)" title="Golden age (metaphor)">classic</a> and recent hit movies, as well as HBO's original specials (which were branded when broadcast on the channel under the banner "Centerstage", which featured stand-up comedy, concert specials and <a href="/wiki/Ice_skating" title="Ice skating">ice skating</a> shows) and documentaries.
</p><p>Festival, whose on-air slogan was <i>Quality Entertainment You Welcome Home</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> had also broadcast collections of feature films featuring a particular movie star (known as "Star Salutes"). What differed Festival from HBO was that the former channel was programmed as a family-oriented service. Atypical for a premium service, Festival featured edited versions of R-rated movies that were recut in order to fit a PG rating and allowed only high-quality series, specials and movies to be broadcast on the channel's schedule.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As Festival was designed as a mini-pay premium service (formatted similarly to Take 2 before it), the cost of a monthly subscription of the channel was also priced lower than that of HBO and Cinemax. Festival provided its subscribers with a color 20-page monthly program guide. Like HBO, Festival also ran occasional free preview periods, such as the October 30 to November 2, 1987 preview hosted by <a href="/wiki/Tony_Randall" title="Tony Randall">Tony Randall</a>. However, the channel suffered from insufficient cable carriage as only a few providers carried Festival; as such, it could not compete with then-fellow premium service <a href="/wiki/Disney_Channel" title="Disney Channel">The Disney Channel</a>, which also maintained a family-oriented programming format (that service would convert into a basic cable channel in April 1997). Festival would eventually shut down in late 1988<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="Statement needs to be more specific about the content to which it refers. (May 2016)">specify</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-HBO_Expanding_the_Brand_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBO_Expanding_the_Brand-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Festival_program_guide_1987_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Festival_program_guide_1987-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="The_Comedy_Channel_.2F_Comedy_Central"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Comedy_Channel_/_Comedy_Central">The Comedy Channel / Comedy Central</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: The Comedy Channel / Comedy Central">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/The_Comedy_Channel_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Comedy Channel (United States)">The Comedy Channel (United States)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Comedy_Central" title="Comedy Central">Comedy Central</a></div>
<p>In 1989, HBO created The Comedy Channel, a <a href="/wiki/Cable_channel" class="mw-redirect" title="Cable channel">basic cable channel</a> that featured clips excerpted from stand-up comedy sets, comedic feature films and television series (using a programming model similar to the original format of MTV),<sup id="cite_ref-HBO_Expanding_the_Brand_41-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBO_Expanding_the_Brand-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> which launched on November 15 of that year. The channel competed with another startup comedy-oriented cable channel that debuted the following year, Viacom-owned <a href="/wiki/Ha!_(TV_channel)" title="Ha! (TV channel)">Ha!: The TV Comedy Network</a>, which focused on reruns of older network sitcoms. Both channels suffered from insufficient cable carriage (both Ha! and The Comedy Channel each had fewer than 10 million subscribers). This resulted in Viacom and HBO reaching an agreement to merge Ha! and The Comedy Channel into a single channel called CTV: The Comedy Network, which debuted on April 1, 1991;<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> the channel subsequently changed its name three months later to Comedy Central due to confusion and potential legal issues with Canadian broadcaster, the <a href="/wiki/CTV_Television_Network" title="CTV Television Network">CTV Television Network</a>. Time Warner/HBO exited the venture when Viacom bought out its 50% stake in Comedy Central for $1.23 billion in April 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-cnn03_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnn03-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Television_and_film_production">Television and film production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Television and film production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>HBO formed the production company HBO Independent Productions in 1990, which mainly served to produce sitcoms for broadcast television and basic cable (which included series such as <i><a href="/wiki/Martin_(TV_series)" title="Martin (TV series)">Martin</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Roc_(TV_series)" title="Roc (TV series)">Roc</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ben_Stiller_Show" title="The Ben Stiller Show">The Ben Stiller Show</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Everybody_Loves_Raymond" title="Everybody Loves Raymond">Everybody Loves Raymond</a></i>). <a href="/wiki/HBO_Downtown_Productions" class="mw-redirect" title="HBO Downtown Productions">HBO Downtown Productions</a> was formed one year later, producing comedy specials for HBO, as well as program content for Comedy Central (such as <i><a href="/wiki/Politically_Incorrect_with_Bill_Maher" class="mw-redirect" title="Politically Incorrect with Bill Maher">Politically Incorrect with Bill Maher</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Dr._Katz,_Professional_Therapist" title="Dr. Katz, Professional Therapist">Dr. Katz, Professional Therapist</a></i>).
</p><p>HBO also operates <a href="/wiki/HBO_Films" title="HBO Films">HBO Films</a>, which was established in 1983 as HBO Premiere Films then HBO Pictures, which operate another movie division, HBO NYC Productions, originally HBO Showcase, until subsumed into HBO Films.
</p><p>HBO also participated in a number of joint ventures in film production:
</p>
<ul><li>In 1982, HBO entered into a joint venture with <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Pictures" title="Columbia Pictures">Columbia Pictures</a> and <a href="/wiki/CBS_Theatrical_Films" title="CBS Theatrical Films">CBS Theatrical Films</a> to form <a href="/wiki/TriStar_Pictures" title="TriStar Pictures">Tri-Star Pictures</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Hyphen" title="Hyphen">hyphen</a> was dropped from the name in 1991), in order to pool resources to split the ever-growing costs of making feature films. Tri-Star's first production, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Natural_(film)" title="The Natural (film)">The Natural</a></i>, was released in 1984. CBS sold its ownership stake in the studio in November 1985.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> In April 1987, Tri-Star entered into the television production business with the formation of <a href="/wiki/TriStar_Television" title="TriStar Television">Tri-Star Television</a>. HBO relinquished its stake in Tri-Star that December, with Columbia Pictures buying its venture shares and merging Columbia and <a href="/wiki/TriStar_Pictures" title="TriStar Pictures">Tri-Star</a> into <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Pictures" title="Columbia Pictures">Columbia Pictures Entertainment</a>. As of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, TriStar operates as a production arm of <a href="/wiki/Sony_Pictures" title="Sony Pictures">Sony Pictures Entertainment</a>.</li>
<li>In 1983, HBO entered into a <a href="/wiki/Limited_partnership" title="Limited partnership">limited partnership</a> with <a href="/wiki/Thorn_EMI" title="Thorn EMI">Thorn EMI</a> to create <a href="/wiki/Silver_Screen_Partners" title="Silver Screen Partners">Silver Screen Partners</a>, which was the first LP of its kind that was developed for the purpose of financing the production of feature films. The studio only released seven films between 1983 and 1986, most of which were not commercial or critical successes, with the minor exception of the 1985 comedy film <i><a href="/wiki/Volunteers_(1985_film)" title="Volunteers (1985 film)">Volunteers</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Movie_Duels_125-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Movie_Duels-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1987, HBO entered into another limited partnership to create Cinema Plus L.P. The studios' most notable is a Silver Pictures co-production, <i>Ricochet</i>. Others were <i>Mom and Dad Save the World</i>, <i>Switch</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Don%27t_Tell_Mom_the_Babysitter%27s_Dead" title="Don&#39;t Tell Mom the Babysitter&#39;s Dead">Don't Tell Mom the Babysitter's Dead</a></i>. All were releases between 1991 and 1992 and all of these pictures were distributed by <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros." class="mw-redirect" title="Warner Bros.">Warner Bros.</a>. None of these were critical or commercial success.</li>
<li>In 1993, HBO purchased post theatrical rights for it first 48 films from upstart production company <a href="/wiki/Savoy_Pictures" title="Savoy Pictures">Savoy Pictures</a>, co-founded by Victor A. Kaufman and Lewis J. Korman.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> Savoy Pictures was unable to experience success with any of its feature film releases, and eventually folded in 1997.<sup id="cite_ref-Movie_Duels_125-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Movie_Duels-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>In 2005, HBO Films and New Line Cinema formed <a href="/wiki/Picturehouse_(company)" title="Picturehouse (company)">Picturehouse</a>, a worldwide theatrical distribution company for high-quality <a href="/wiki/Independent_film" title="Independent film">independent films</a>. The company was shut down in 2008 as part of the consolidation of New Line with its sister studio Warner Bros. Entertainment (Picturehouse CEO Bob Berney would later resurrect the studio as an independent entity from <a href="/wiki/Time_Warner" class="mw-redirect" title="Time Warner">Time Warner</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Movie_Duels_125-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Movie_Duels-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Home_video">Home video</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HBO&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Home video">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="infobox vcard" style="width:22em"><caption class="fn org">HBO Home Entertainment</caption><tbody><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">Formerly</div></th><td class="nickname" style="line-height: 1.35em;">Thorn EMI Video (1980-1985)<br />Thorn EMI/HBO Video (1985-86)<br />HBO/Cannon Video (1986-87)<br />HBO Video (1987-1993; 2003-2009)<br />HBO Home Video/HBO Savoy Home Video (1994-2003)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;"><a Wikidata"><img alt="Edit this on Wikidata" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8a/OOjs_UI_icon_edit-ltr-progressive.svg/10px-OOjs_UI_icon_edit-ltr-progressive.svg.png" decoding="async" States</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;">Products</th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;">Home video releases</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;">Services</th><td class="category" style="line-height: 1.35em;"><a href="/wiki/Home_video" title="Home video">Home video</a><br /><a href="/wiki/Digital_distribution" title="Digital
